Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
8 min
The Necessity of the Concomitant for Demonstration
Author argues that knowledge of the concomitant is necessary for demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Razi's argument that knowledge of the necessary concomitant of a quiddity is essential for valid demonstration, and defend it against the infinite regress objection.

Core Idea
The concomitant is the bridge from known to unknown in demonstration.

In this passage from al-Mulakhkhas, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles a foundational issue: how can a demonstration yield new knowledge? The answer lies in the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) of the quiddity, which serves as the middle term linking subject and predicate. Razi argues that without this concomitant, demonstration collapses. He then addresses a subtle objection about infinite regress. Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Razi states that if knowledge of the quiddity did not entail knowledge of its necessary concomitant, then deriving an unknown proposition from two known premises would be impossible.
This conditional is the core of the argument.
2
Explain why the predicate must be extrinsic
In a valid demonstrative conclusion, the predicate cannot be part of the subject's quiddity; otherwise it would be already known and no demonstration would be needed.
3
Locate the middle term
The necessary concomitant (lazim) functions as the middle term in the syllogism. Knowledge of the quiddity leads to knowledge of the concomitant, which then links to the predicate.
4
Understand the regress objection
An objector says: if the concomitant is evident, then its own concomitant would also be evident, leading to an infinite chain and making all concomitants known.
This would undermine the need for demonstration.
5
Analyze Razi's response
Razi replies that the concomitant is only evident when the subject is fully conceptualized in the mind. The concomitant itself is known through itself, not through another concomitant, thus blocking the regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا تكون المقدمة مقضية هذا خلف أو لا يكون وذلك في الملزوم بالكلية جن إثبات لازم دنى وسطا هنا يا وجعين أن يكون الماهية مقضية صفة وهي صفة الغريب أن تكون الماهية مقضية صفة ثم إنها مع تلك الصفة فتكون صفة أخرى ولا امتناع في واحد منها كية أن كل من صور الماهية فإنه لا بد وأن يعقل لأنها الغرب قبل لأن الماهية علة للأنجبا الغرب والعلم بالعلم علة للعلم بالمعلول والعالم المستقصى فيه الحكمة والأقوى أن يقال لو لم يلزم من العلم بالماهية العلم بلازمها الغرب لاستحال تعرف القضية المجهولة من مقدمتين معلومتين وفساد الثاني يدل على افساد المقدم بيان الشرطية. إن محمول المنتجة لا بد وأن يكون خارجا عن ماهية الموضوع وإلا لم يكن مجهول البرهان له وإنما يكون كذلك لو كان خارجا عن ماهية الواسطة أو كانت الواسطة خارجة عن ماهية الموضوع وإلا كان محمول المنتجة من جزء موضوعها فيكون جوا أو يعود المحال وإذا كان كذلك فلو لم تجبر الوصف الخارجي الغريب أن يكون بين البيوت لله الحق كل واحدة من المقدستين المذكورتين أو إحداهما إلى متين آخر وذلك أما القياس ويكون العلام فيه كالكلام في الأول ولزم السلا والحشر وهو محال لأنه لا يعطى المقدمة الكلية على ما ستعرفة مثبت أنه لو لم يكن اللازم الغريب للماهية بين الشوق لها لاستحال تعرف القضية المجهولة لا يقال لازم اللام لازم قرب لذلك اللازم فلو كان اللازم بين البنفس للملزوم لزم من العلم به العلم بلازم اللازم لزم أن يكون جميع اللوازم يقينة لأنا نقول أنا لا ندعي أن كان لازم قريب منو بين النشوف للملزوم إلا بشرط جمهور تصوره في الذهن ولما حجب ذلك لم يبحث كون اللوازم باسرها بينة في أن اللازم ذل الوسط / المكن العباء شوقه إلا بواسطة العلم بذلك الوسط قبل لأن اللازم من لذاته والجواب
So the premise would not be decided, this is a contradiction or it is not, and that is in the [notion of] necessary concomitance in universality. For establishing a necessary concomitant as a middle term here... that the quiddity is a decided attribute, and it is the attribute of the strange [i.e., remote?] that the quiddity is a decided attribute, then with that attribute it becomes another attribute, and there is no impossibility in any of them. For every form of quiddity must be understood because it is the strange before, because the quiddity is the cause of the strangeness, and knowledge of the cause is a cause for knowledge of the effect, and the investigator who is thorough in wisdom. The stronger is to say: if knowledge of the quiddity did not entail knowledge of its necessary concomitant, then it would be impossible to know an unknown proposition from two known premises. The corruption of the second indicates the corruption of the first. Explanation of the conditional: the predicate of the conclusion must be outside the quiddity of the subject, otherwise it would not be unknown to the demonstration. It is only like that if it is outside the quiddity of the middle [term] or the middle is outside the quiddity of the subject; otherwise the predicate of the conclusion would be part of the subject's quiddity, so it would be either [already known] or the impossible would occur. And if that is so, then if the external description [i.e., the concomitant] were not compelled to be between... for God's truth, each of the two mentioned premises or one of them to another text. That is either a syllogism, and the discussion therein is like the discussion in the first, and [we] would fall into infinite regress and accumulation, which is impossible because it does not yield a universal premise as will be learned. It establishes that if the remote necessary concomitant of the quiddity were not evident, it would be impossible to know an unknown proposition. It cannot be said: the necessary concomitant of the necessary concomitant is a close necessary concomitant; if the necessary concomitant is essentially evident to the [subject], then from knowledge of it comes knowledge of the necessary concomitant of the necessary concomitant, which would make all necessary concomitants certain. For we say: we do not claim that any close necessary concomitant is evident to the subject except under the condition of its being fully conceptualized in the mind. When that is prevented, the question of whether all concomitants are evident does not arise. For the necessary concomitant is the middle term. It is not possible to know it except through knowledge of that middle term, because the necessary concomitant is [known] through itself. And the answer [is as above].
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration of the epistemological necessity of knowing the concomitant (lazim) of a quiddity for the validity of demonstration (burhan). The author argues that if the concomitant were not known, deriving a new unknown proposition from two known premises would be impossible. He explains that the predicate of a demonstrative conclusion must be extrinsic to the subject's quiddity; otherwise it would be part of the subject and thus already known. This extrinsic predicate is the concomitant that serves as the middle term. The author refutes a possible objection that if the concomitant were evident, then its own concomitant would also be evident ad infinitum, by clarifying that the concomitant is evident only when the subject is fully conceived in the mind. The passage underscores the role of the middle term as the necessary link between subject and predicate in demonstration.

Key Terms
اللازم
الماهية
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the predicate of a demonstrative conclusion be extrinsic to the subject's quiddity?
A) Because otherwise it would be unknown
B) Because otherwise it would be part of the subject and thus already known, making demonstration pointless
C) Because the predicate must be a concomitant of the subject
D) Because the subject must be simple

Key Takeaway
The necessary concomitant of a quiddity is indispensable as the middle term in demonstration, allowing the derivation of new knowledge from known premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the demonstration that the Quran is the word of Allah. Identify what the middle term (concomitant) would be, and explain why it must be extrinsic to the quiddity of the Quran.
?
Construct a simple categorical syllogism in the first figure where the middle term is a necessary concomitant of the subject, using an example from fiqh (e.g., that every intoxicant is forbidden, and nabidh is an intoxicant).

Sensory Particulars vs. Universal Intellectual Principles
Author argues sensory premises cannot provide universal propositions needed for syllogism; primary propositions are foundational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sensory particulars from universal judgments and identify primary propositions (awwaliyat) using the thought experiment of stripping all habits.

Core Idea
Sense gives only 'this fire is hot'; intellect gives 'all fire is hot'.

This lesson tackles a foundational epistemological question: can sense perception alone provide the universal premises needed for syllogisms? The author argues it cannot—sense only delivers particular facts. Universal judgments require intellectual principles, specifically the primary propositions (awwaliyat) that are self-evident. He then introduces a method to distinguish truly self-evident propositions from merely widely accepted ones (mashhurat) or imaginary ones (wahmiyat).

Guided Path
1
Identify what sense perception provides
The passage states that sense only informs about particular instances: 'this tangible fire is hot', 'this sun at this hour is luminous'. It does not give universal statements like 'every fire is hot' or 'every sun is luminous'. Sense is confined to the here and now.
The author emphasizes that even if we could sense all past, present, and future instances, that would still not yield a necessary universal proposition.
2
Explain why sensory propositions are insufficient for syllogism
A syllogism requires at least one universal premise (qadiyya kulliyya). Since sense only yields particulars, sensory propositions alone cannot form a valid syllogism. The syllogism's conclusion would lack universality and necessity.
Recall that in categorical syllogisms, the major premise must be universal (e.g., Barbara mood). Without it, no conclusion follows.
3
Recognize the intellect as the source of universals
The author states that 'the primary giver of universal propositions is the intellect'. The first principles (awwaliyat) are self-evident propositions that the intellect assents to immediately, without any prior knowledge or sensory input.
Examples in Islamic sciences: 'The whole is greater than its part' (kulliyat), or in kalam: 'The world is originated' requires an intellectual first principle.
4
Apply the thought experiment to distinguish primary from accepted propositions
To tell whether a proposition is awwali (primary) or mashhur (accepted), imagine yourself stripped of all theoretical and practical habits, as if created now with no prior experience. If in this state you judge the proposition immediately (without hesitation), it is primary. If you hesitate, it is merely accepted opinion.
The author also notes that wahmiyat (imaginaries) are propositions where both imagination and intellect agree but may be false; their falsity is known when imagination contradicts intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسية مبادىء غالية بل عالم تستند إلى العقل ولم تصرف العقل فيها بالمنين والتقدلم بين الأعتماد عليهات إن الحس إنما يفيد كون هذه النار الملموسة حارة وكون هذه المرة هذه الساعة منيرة فاما ان كل نار حارة و كل شمس مضيئة فكر جم مع جهة فذلك ما لا تناولة الحس انا وان قدرنا الأحساس مجمع الجنيات التي وجدت في الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل وإن كان ذلك ممتعا لكن ذلك لا يعطى القضية الخلية واذا ثبت هذ الم تكن القضايا الحسبة قافية بتركب القياس لان الأقلية لابد فيها من للقضية الحملة فقطعَانٌ مَدَاء المبادئ في اعطاء القضايا الحلية التقنية في الفقة العقلية وان لوّل الا قاتل في القضايا هي الأوليات وإن ما عدلها ستفتح عليها ثم هنا إشكالات فانع الحكماءات كثيرا من الوهمات والمشهورات في قوة هذه الأوليات أما الوهميات فقد تكون كادية وإنما يعرف كن بها لتطابق الوهم والعقل على التصديق بأمورٍ يلزم من التصديق بالمهر اعلام من التصديق بها كتب تلك القحبات فلا اعتنق الوهم ما أوجب لقيض حكمي عرف كذبه واما المشهورات فانانات الاوليات عنها بانا نفرض انفنا مجردة عن جميع المئات النظرية والعملية وكانا خلقنا الآن دفعة من عز ان شاهدنا لحدا و لا مارينا عملا ثم عَضَاعَ الفنا قضية فان وجدنا انشاف هذه الحالة جَائِمَةً تها انها الفنية فإن توقفت في هذه الحالة علنا ان القضية مشهورة والتى تعب اليه نحن إله الجوز أن يكون عبر الاقليات في قوتها السنة إذ لو كان كذلك لان الميز بينها وبين عنها لابد وان تكون مطابق آخر و دار جزم النَّهِن بها فيكون التميز بينها وبين غيرها
Sensory principles are high? but a world based on intellect, and the intellect did not dispose them... Indeed, sense only informs that this tangible fire is hot and that this sun at this hour is luminous. As for that every fire is hot and every sun is luminous, that is a universal judgment, and sense does not grasp that. Even if we supposed sense to gather all instances that existed in the past, present, and future, though that is impossible, still it would not yield the universal proposition. When this is established, sensory propositions are not sufficient for the composition of syllogism, because the universal judgment is necessary in it... The primary giver of universal propositions is the intellect. The first of propositions are the primary ones, and those after them are based on them. Then there are problems: many of the wise [؟] consider the imaginaries and accepted opinions to be in the strength of these primary ones. As for the imaginaries, they may be [؟] and they are known by the agreement of imagination and intellect on assenting to matters from the assent to which... The imagination does not [؟] what necessitates a judgment whose falsehood is known. As for the accepted opinions, we distinguish them from primary ones by supposing ourselves stripped of all theoretical and practical habits, as if we were created now suddenly, having seen nothing and practiced no action, then we consider a proposition: if we find that in this state we judge it, it is primary; if we hesitate in this state, we know the proposition is accepted. And what we intend [؟] is that the primary ones may surpass in strength...
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological foundation of certain knowledge in demonstration. The author argues that sensory experience alone cannot provide the universal premises required for syllogisms. Sense perception only gives particular facts, e.g., that this specific fire is hot or this specific sun is luminous. It does not yield universal statements like 'All fire is hot' or 'All suns are luminous.' Even if we could sense all individual instances across time, that would not give a necessary universal proposition. Therefore, sensory propositions are insufficient for constructing syllogisms (qiyas). The intellect must supply the universal judgment. The first principles (awwaliyat) are the most basic self-evident propositions, and all other propositions depend on them. Then the author addresses two types of propositions that some philosophers consider as strong as primary ones: wahmiyat (imaginary propositions) and mashhurat (accepted opinions). Wahmiyat are those where both imagination and intellect agree, but the author suggests they may be deceptive. Mashhurat are distinguished by a thought experiment: imagine oneself with no prior knowledge or habits, then consider a proposition. If we judge it immediately, it is primary; if we hesitate, it is accepted opinion. The passage concludes that primary propositions are indeed stronger in certainty.

Key Terms
الحسيات
الأوليات
الوهميات
المشهورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is sense perception insufficient for the universal premise needed in a syllogism?
A) Because sense perception is always deceptive.
B) Because sense perception only provides particular facts, not universal judgments.
C) Because syllogisms never use universal premises.
D) Because sense perception requires prior intellectual principles.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception gives only particular facts; universal premises for syllogism must come from intellectual first principles (awwaliyat).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the thought experiment from the passage, determine whether the proposition 'the whole is greater than its part' is a primary (awwali) or accepted (mashhur) proposition. Justify your answer.
?
Explain why the statement 'this specific fire in front of me is hot' cannot serve as the major premise in a categorical syllogism proving that all fire is hot. What must be added to make it a valid syllogism?

Why Sensation Cannot Give Universal Certainty
Author critiques sensory premises as source of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why sensory premises alone are insufficient for constructing a demonstration (burhan) in the Islamic theological sciences.

Core Idea
Sensation yields particulars; universal certainty requires self-evident first principles.

The author begins by challenging the claim that sensory experience can serve as a foundation for certain knowledge. He argues that while our senses tell us about specific instances (e.g., this fire is hot), they cannot establish universal propositions (e.g., every fire is hot). This is a crucial point because the syllogisms used in kalam and usul al-fiqh depend on universal premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two capacities of sense perception
The author distinguishes between what sense can tell us about particular instances and what it cannot tell us about universal truths. Note the examples: 'this fire is hot' vs. 'every fire is hot'.
2
Understand the argument against universal generalization from sensation
Even if sensation could cover all past, present, and future particulars, it would not produce a universal proposition because the universal judgment goes beyond the sum of particulars.
This is a classical critique of induction.
3
Connect to the syllogism requirement
The author concludes that sensory propositions are insufficient for the composition of a syllogism because syllogisms require universal premises.
In Islamic logic, the burhan (demonstration) must have universal premises.
4
Identify the alternative source
The author states that the true principles giving universal certain propositions are the 'first principles' (awwaliyat).
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسية مبادئ غالية بل علوم تستند إلى العقل ولم تصرف العقل فيها بالمنين والتقديم بين الأعتماد عليهات إن الحس إنما يفيد كون هذه النار الملموسة حارة وكون هذه المرة هذه الساعة منيرة فأما أن كل نار حارة وكل شمس مضيئة فذلك ما لا تتناوله الحس. وإن قدرنا الإحساس مجمع الجزئيات التي وجدت في الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل وإن كان ذلك ممكنا لكن ذلك لا يعطي القضية الكلية. وإذا ثبت هذا لم تكن القضايا الحسية كافية بتركيب القياس لأن الكلية لابد فيها من القضية الكلية فقط. والمبادئ في إعطاء القضايا الكلية اليقينية في العلوم العقلية هي الأوليات. ثم هنا إشكالات: فإن الحكماء يأخذون كثيرا من الوهميات والمشهورات في قوة هذه الأوليات. أما الوهميات فإما تكون كاذبة وإنما يعرف كذبها لتطابق الوهم والعقل على التصديق بأمور يلزم من التصديق بها علم من التصديق بها. وأما المشهورات فمعرفة الأوليات عنها بأن نفرض أنفسنا مجردة عن جميع الميول النظرية والعملية وكأنا خلقنا الآن دفعة من غير أن شاهدنا أحدا ولا مارسنا عملا ثم عرضنا على أنفسنا قضية فإن وجدنا أنفسنا في هذه الحالة جازمة بها فهي أولية وإن توقفت في هذه الحالة علمنا أن القضية مشهورة. والمنتهى إلى أن يكون للأوليات في قوتها سنة إذ لو كان كذلك لكان الميز بينها وبين غيرها لابد وأن يكون مطابقا آخر. والجزم بها فيكون التميز بينها وبين غيرها ممتنعا. ثم هب لنا أن قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين وما يجري مجراه علم ضروري غير قابل للاحتمال لكنكم في المباحث الحكمية والطبيعية والالهية لا تستعملون هذه المقدمات بل تستعملون مقدمات أخر كقولكم الممكن المشاوي لا يتح احد هل فيها الآخر. وإذا انتهى كلامكم إلى هذه المقدمات وأشباهها ادعيتم الضرورة والجزم ومعلوم أن هذه القضايا ليست بقوة قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين. ولذلك إذا عرضا على العقل لم يكن الجزم بأحدهما كالجزم بالآخر. والتفاوت في العلوم اليقينية غير جائز لأنه إن حصل الجزم ولو على أبعد الوجوه حصل اليقين وإلا لم يحصل التفاوت أصلا. فعلم أن المقدمات المستعملة في هذه العلوم غير يقينية. ولذلك نقل بعض المتأخرين عن أرسطو أنه قال الأمور الإلهية لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين منها. ثم إن جمهور المنطقيين اعتبروا في البرهان الاعتقاد المطابق والجازم ثانيا وهو اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون الأمر إلا كما اعتقدوه. فالبرهان يقتضي اعتبار هذا الاعتقاد الثاني وإلا لوجب أن يكون اعتقاد المقارن علما. وإذا ثبت ذلك فنقول ذلك الاعتقاد الثاني إن كان يقينا وجب أن يحصل فيه اعتقاد ثالث متعلق بأن الأمر ليس إلا كما تعلق الاعتقاد الثاني ويدخل الكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول ويلزم التسلسل. وإن لم يكن يقينا والاعتقاد الأول إنما يصير يقينا عند حصوله والمبني على ما لا يكون يقينيا لا يكون يقينيا فوجب أن لا يحصل اليقين في شيء من الاعتقادات وذلك قادح.
Sensory principles are lofty? Rather, sciences rely on the intellect, and the intellect does not dispose them through composition and evaluation? But reliance on them? Sense perception only informs that this tangible fire is hot and that this sun at this hour is luminous. As for that every fire is hot and every sun is luminous, that is not attained by sense. Even if we suppose sensation to include all particulars that existed in the past, present, and future, and that is possible, that does not give the universal proposition. If this is established, then sensory propositions are not sufficient for the composition of the syllogism because the universal proposition is inevitably required. The principles that yield universal certain propositions in the intellectual sciences are the 'first principles' (awwaliyat). Then here are problems: the philosophers often take many 'estimative propositions' (wahmiyyat) and 'widely accepted propositions' (mashhurat) to be as strong as these first principles. As for the estimative ones, they may be false and their falsehood is known by the agreement of estimation and intellect upon assenting to things whose assent entails knowledge? As for the widely accepted ones, the knowledge of first principles from them is that we suppose ourselves stripped of all theoretical and practical inclinations, as if we were created just now without having witnessed anyone nor practiced any action, then we present a proposition to our minds; if we find ourselves in this state certain of it, it is a first principle; if we hesitate in this state, we know that the proposition is widely accepted. The outcome is that the first principles have a fixed power? If that were the case, then the distinction between them and others would necessarily be through another criterion. And certainty in them would make the distinction between them and others impossible. Then grant us that our statement 'the one is half of two' and what runs its course is necessary knowledge admitting no probability; but you in the discussions of wisdom and natural and divine affairs do not use these premises, but rather use other premises such as your statement 'the possible qua possible does not entail one of the two?'. And when your discourse ends with these premises and their likes, you claim necessity and certainty. It is obvious that these propositions are not as strong as our statement 'the one is half of two'. Therefore, if they are presented to the mind, certainty in one of them is not like certainty in the other. Disparity in certain sciences is not permissible because if certainty is attained, even in the most remote ways, certainty is actualized; otherwise, no disparity occurs at all. Thus it is known that the premises used in these sciences are not certain. Therefore, some later scholars transmitted from Aristotle that he said: 'Divine matters cannot attain certainty from them.' Then, indeed the majority of logicians considered in demonstration the belief that corresponds and is certain, and secondly the belief that the matter cannot be otherwise than as they believed. So demonstration requires consideration of this second belief; otherwise, the belief of the person in correspondence would have to be knowledge. If this is established, we say that second belief: if it is certain, then a third belief must arise concerning that the matter is only as the second belief pertains, and the discussion in it is like the discussion in the first, leading to an infinite regress. And if it is not certain, and the first belief only becomes certain upon its attainment, and what is based on what is not certain cannot be certain, then it is necessary that certainty is not attained in any of the beliefs, and that is a defect.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a critical epistemological argument. The author begins by asserting that sensory perception only provides knowledge of particular instances (e.g., this fire is hot now) and cannot yield universal propositions (e.g., every fire is hot). Even if sensation could encompass all past, present, and future particulars, it still would not produce a universal judgment. Therefore, sensory premises are insufficient for constructing syllogisms, which require universal premises. The true principles for universal certain knowledge are the 'first principles' (awwaliyat), which are self-evident. However, the author notes a problem: philosophers often treat 'estimative propositions' (wahmiyyat) and 'widely accepted propositions' (mashhurat) as having the same certainty as first principles. He explains that estimative propositions may be false and are recognized as false when estimation and intellect agree on other truths. Widely accepted propositions are distinguished from first principles by a thought experiment: if we imagine ourselves stripped of all biases and prior experiences, and we are certain of a proposition, it is a first principle; if we hesitate, it is widely accepted. The author argues that if first principles had a fixed degree of certainty, there would be no criterion to distinguish them from others, leading to an impossibility. He then challenges the logicians who claim that the premises used in metaphysical, natural, and divine sciences are as certain as 'the one is half of two'. He says they do not actually use such self-evident premises but instead use statements like 'the possible as possible does not entail one of the two', which are not equally certain. The difference in certainty is problematic because if certainty is attained, even with the weakest certainty, it is still certainty; otherwise, there is no certainty at all. Thus, the premises in these sciences are not certain. He cites Aristotle (via later scholars) as saying that divine matters admit no certainty. Then the author critiques the logicians' definition of demonstration as requiring both a certain belief that corresponds to reality and a second-order belief that the matter cannot be otherwise. He argues that this second belief itself must be certain, leading to an infinite regress (a third belief about the second, etc.), or if it is not certain, then the first belief cannot be certain either, since what is based on what is not certain is not certain. This undermines the possibility of certainty in any belief, which is a fatal objection to the logicians' theory.

الحسية

Checkpoint

A) Because we cannot sense all fires.

D) Because the intellect cannot process sensory data.

Key Takeaway
Sensory perception alone cannot provide the universal premises required for demonstration; only self-evident first principles can.

Distinguishing First Principles from Estimative and Widely Accepted Propositions
Author offers a test to distinguish first principles from other propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the thought experiment to distinguish awwaliyat from wahmiyyat and mashhurat.

Core Idea
First principles survive stripping away all biases; other propositions falter.

In this section, the author establishes a criterion for identifying true first principles (awwaliyat). He critiques philosophers who treat estimative propositions (wahmiyyat) and widely accepted propositions (mashhurat) as equivalent in certainty to awwaliyat. He then provides a thought experiment to test any proposition: imagine yourself devoid of all prior experiences and inclinations. If you still affirm it with certainty, it is a first principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of propositions
The author mentions three: first principles (awwaliyat), estimative (wahmiyyat), and widely accepted (mashhurat). Note that philosophers often treat the latter two as certain.
2
Understand the thought experiment
The author asks us to suppose ourselves stripped of all theoretical and practical inclinations, as if created anew. Then present the proposition to yourself. If you are certain without hesitation, it is a first principle. If you hesitate, it is widely accepted.
This is a test for universality and self-evidence.
3
Apply the test
Take examples like 'the whole is greater than the part' (awwaliya) versus 'justice is good' (mashhura). Under the thought experiment, the first would be affirmed immediately, the second might depend on cultural or personal factors.
4
Recognize the problem
The author argues that if first principles had a fixed degree of certainty, there would be no criterion to distinguish them from others, leading to an impossibility. This suggests that the distinction is inherent and not based on a separate criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسية مبادئ غالية بل علوم تستند إلى العقل ولم تصرف العقل فيها بالمنين والتقديم بين الأعتماد عليهات إن الحس إنما يفيد كون هذه النار الملموسة حارة وكون هذه المرة هذه الساعة منيرة فأما أن كل نار حارة وكل شمس مضيئة فذلك ما لا تتناوله الحس. وإن قدرنا الإحساس مجمع الجزئيات التي وجدت في الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل وإن كان ذلك ممكنا لكن ذلك لا يعطي القضية الكلية. وإذا ثبت هذا لم تكن القضايا الحسية كافية بتركيب القياس لأن الكلية لابد فيها من القضية الكلية فقط. والمبادئ في إعطاء القضايا الكلية اليقينية في العلوم العقلية هي الأوليات. ثم هنا إشكالات: فإن الحكماء يأخذون كثيرا من الوهميات والمشهورات في قوة هذه الأوليات. أما الوهميات فإما تكون كاذبة وإنما يعرف كذبها لتطابق الوهم والعقل على التصديق بأمور يلزم من التصديق بها علم من التصديق بها. وأما المشهورات فمعرفة الأوليات عنها بأن نفرض أنفسنا مجردة عن جميع الميول النظرية والعملية وكأنا خلقنا الآن دفعة من غير أن شاهدنا أحدا ولا مارسنا عملا ثم عرضنا على أنفسنا قضية فإن وجدنا أنفسنا في هذه الحالة جازمة بها فهي أولية وإن توقفت في هذه الحالة علمنا أن القضية مشهورة. والمنتهى إلى أن يكون للأوليات في قوتها سنة إذ لو كان كذلك لكان الميز بينها وبين غيرها لابد وأن يكون مطابقا آخر. والجزم بها فيكون التميز بينها وبين غيرها ممتنعا. ثم هب لنا أن قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين وما يجري مجراه علم ضروري غير قابل للاحتمال لكنكم في المباحث الحكمية والطبيعية والالهية لا تستعملون هذه المقدمات بل تستعملون مقدمات أخر كقولكم الممكن المشاوي لا يتح احد هل فيها الآخر. وإذا انتهى كلامكم إلى هذه المقدمات وأشباهها ادعيتم الضرورة والجزم ومعلوم أن هذه القضايا ليست بقوة قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين. ولذلك إذا عرضا على العقل لم يكن الجزم بأحدهما كالجزم بالآخر. والتفاوت في العلوم اليقينية غير جائز لأنه إن حصل الجزم ولو على أبعد الوجوه حصل اليقين وإلا لم يحصل التفاوت أصلا. فعلم أن المقدمات المستعملة في هذه العلوم غير يقينية. ولذلك نقل بعض المتأخرين عن أرسطو أنه قال الأمور الإلهية لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين منها. ثم إن جمهور المنطقيين اعتبروا في البرهان الاعتقاد المطابق والجازم ثانيا وهو اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون الأمر إلا كما اعتقدوه. فالبرهان يقتضي اعتبار هذا الاعتقاد الثاني وإلا لوجب أن يكون اعتقاد المقارن علما. وإذا ثبت ذلك فنقول ذلك الاعتقاد الثاني إن كان يقينا وجب أن يحصل فيه اعتقاد ثالث متعلق بأن الأمر ليس إلا كما تعلق الاعتقاد الثاني ويدخل الكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول ويلزم التسلسل. وإن لم يكن يقينا والاعتقاد الأول إنما يصير يقينا عند حصوله والمبني على ما لا يكون يقينيا لا يكون يقينيا فوجب أن لا يحصل اليقين في شيء من الاعتقادات وذلك قادح.
Sensory principles are lofty? Rather, sciences rely on the intellect, and the intellect does not dispose them through composition and evaluation? But reliance on them? Sense perception only informs that this tangible fire is hot and that this sun at this hour is luminous. As for that every fire is hot and every sun is luminous, that is not attained by sense. Even if we suppose sensation to include all particulars that existed in the past, present, and future, and that is possible, that does not give the universal proposition. If this is established, then sensory propositions are not sufficient for the composition of the syllogism because the universal proposition is inevitably required. The principles that yield universal certain propositions in the intellectual sciences are the 'first principles' (awwaliyat). Then here are problems: the philosophers often take many 'estimative propositions' (wahmiyyat) and 'widely accepted propositions' (mashhurat) to be as strong as these first principles. As for the estimative ones, they may be false and their falsehood is known by the agreement of estimation and intellect upon assenting to things whose assent entails knowledge? As for the widely accepted ones, the knowledge of first principles from them is that we suppose ourselves stripped of all theoretical and practical inclinations, as if we were created just now without having witnessed anyone nor practiced any action, then we present a proposition to our minds; if we find ourselves in this state certain of it, it is a first principle; if we hesitate in this state, we know that the proposition is widely accepted. The outcome is that the first principles have a fixed power? If that were the case, then the distinction between them and others would necessarily be through another criterion. And certainty in them would make the distinction between them and others impossible. Then grant us that our statement 'the one is half of two' and what runs its course is necessary knowledge admitting no probability; but you in the discussions of wisdom and natural and divine affairs do not use these premises, but rather use other premises such as your statement 'the possible qua possible does not entail one of the two?'. And when your discourse ends with these premises and their likes, you claim necessity and certainty. It is obvious that these propositions are not as strong as our statement 'the one is half of two'. Therefore, if they are presented to the mind, certainty in one of them is not like certainty in the other. Disparity in certain sciences is not permissible because if certainty is attained, even in the most remote ways, certainty is actualized; otherwise, no disparity occurs at all. Thus it is known that the premises used in these sciences are not certain. Therefore, some later scholars transmitted from Aristotle that he said: 'Divine matters cannot attain certainty from them.' Then, indeed the majority of logicians considered in demonstration the belief that corresponds and is certain, and secondly the belief that the matter cannot be otherwise than as they believed. So demonstration requires consideration of this second belief; otherwise, the belief of the person in correspondence would have to be knowledge. If this is established, we say that second belief: if it is certain, then a third belief must arise concerning that the matter is only as the second belief pertains, and the discussion in it is like the discussion in the first, leading to an infinite regress. And if it is not certain, and the first belief only becomes certain upon its attainment, and what is based on what is not certain cannot be certain, then it is necessary that certainty is not attained in any of the beliefs, and that is a defect.

Commentary
This passage is a critical epistemological argument. The author begins by asserting that sensory perception only provides knowledge of particular instances (e.g., this fire is hot now) and cannot yield universal propositions (e.g., every fire is hot). Even if sensation could encompass all past, present, and future particulars, it still would not produce a universal judgment. Therefore, sensory premises are insufficient for constructing syllogisms, which require universal premises. The true principles for universal certain knowledge are the 'first principles' (awwaliyat), which are self-evident. However, the author notes a problem: philosophers often treat 'estimative propositions' (wahmiyyat) and 'widely accepted propositions' (mashhurat) as having the same certainty as first principles. He explains that estimative propositions may be false and are recognized as false when estimation and intellect agree on other truths. Widely accepted propositions are distinguished from first principles by a thought experiment: if we imagine ourselves stripped of all biases and prior experiences, and we are certain of a proposition, it is a first principle; if we hesitate, it is widely accepted. The author argues that if first principles had a fixed degree of certainty, there would be no criterion to distinguish them from others, leading to an impossibility. He then challenges the logicians who claim that the premises used in metaphysical, natural, and divine sciences are as certain as 'the one is half of two'. He says they do not actually use such self-evident premises but instead use statements like 'the possible as possible does not entail one of the two', which are not equally certain. The difference in certainty is problematic because if certainty is attained, even with the weakest certainty, it is still certainty; otherwise, there is no certainty at all. Thus, the premises in these sciences are not certain. He cites Aristotle (via later scholars) as saying that divine matters admit no certainty. Then the author critiques the logicians' definition of demonstration as requiring both a certain belief that corresponds to reality and a second-order belief that the matter cannot be otherwise. He argues that this second belief itself must be certain, leading to an infinite regress (a third belief about the second, etc.), or if it is not certain, then the first belief cannot be certain either, since what is based on what is not certain is not certain. This undermines the possibility of certainty in any belief, which is a fatal objection to the logicians' theory.

The Infinite Regress Argument Against Certainty in Divine Sciences
Author critiques the certainty of premises in divine sciences and the logicians' definition of demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the infinite regress argument against the logicians' definition of demonstration (burhan).

Core Idea
Demanding second-order certainty leads to an infinite regress, collapsing the possibility of certainty.

In this final part, the author delivers a devastating critique. He challenges the claim that the premises used in metaphysical and divine sciences are as certain as 'the one is half of two'. He then attacks the logicians' requirement that demonstration includes both belief in correspondence and belief that the matter cannot be otherwise. He shows this leads to an infinite regress or defeats certainty altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being challenged
The author challenges the idea that premises in divine sciences are as certain as self-evident truths like 'the one is half of two'. He notes that philosophers use premises like 'the possible qua possible does not entail one of the two', which are not equally evident.
2
Understand the objection about degrees of certainty
The author argues that if certainty is present, even with the weakest degree, it is still certainty. So disparity in certainty is problematic because either you have certainty or you don't. The premises in divine sciences are therefore not certain.
3
Follow the infinite regress argument
Logicians define demonstration as requiring: (1) belief that corresponds to reality and (2) belief that the matter cannot be otherwise. The author asks: is the second belief certain? If it is certain, then we need a third belief about it, and so on infinitely. If it is not certain, then the first belief cannot be certain either, because what is based on what is not certain cannot be certain.
This is a powerful regress argument.
4
Conclude the implication
Therefore, either no certainty can be attained, or the logicians' definition is flawed. This undermines the claim that divine sciences can achieve certainty through demonstration.

Core Argument: Instant Separation Entails Void
Demonstrative proof that instant separation necessitates void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical steps of the argument that instant surface separation necessarily produces a void.

Core Idea
Two surfaces cannot separate instantly without creating a void – otherwise absurdities follow.

In this passage, the author presents a demonstrative argument (burhan) to prove that if two surfaces separate in a single instant, a void must appear between them. This is a key claim in Islamic physics, used to reject the possibility of void. We will unpack the logical structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Find the sentence that states the necessary conclusion: 'if one surface contacts another and then rises from it at once, then the occurrence of a void between them at the time of rising is necessary.' This is the claim being proved.
The thesis is a conditional: if P (instant separation) then Q (void exists).
2
Extract the proof structure
The author uses a reductio: he assumes the opposite (no void) and shows it leads to absurdities like overlapping points or interpenetration of bodies. Trace this line: if no void, then the surfaces either separate as whole surfaces (which gives void) or as points (which gives overlapping).
Overlapping of points is impossible, so only the surface separation remains, which implies void.
3
Analyze the refutation of objections
The author considers porous bodies and sideways motion as potential counterexamples. He shows that porous bodies still have continuous surfaces, and sideways motion would lead to circularity or interpenetration. Identify these objections and the author's responses.
These refutations strengthen the original thesis by showing alternative mechanisms are impossible.
4
Formalize the argument
Write the main deduction in conditional form: If (surfaces separate at once) then (void is created). The proof shows that the negation of void leads to contradiction, so the conditional must be true. This is a proof by conditional proof.
This is a standard logical technique in Islamic logic: establishing a conditional via reductio.

The strongest of proofs are two ways: If one surface contacts another surface and then rises from it at once, then the occurrence of a void between them at the time of rising is necessary. Let us examine the possibility of this. As for the claim that a surface must meet a surface? Because if it were impossible, then the void would be obviously necessary. And also that... For if we place the inside of our finger on another body, we know intuitively that it cannot be said that contact did not occur except by a point in our finger and a point in that body. And the second, regarding whether it moves to the side of the body, either to its place or to another side. The second is false because the motion of a body from its place depends on the vacuity of the place to which it moves; so if each one moves to the place of the other, then the motion of each depends on the motion of the other, leading to circularity. And the second [؟] the difficulty in how that body moves like the difficulty in the motion of the first body; so the interpenetration of all bodies would continue until [؟] from that [؟] the motion of the heavens, and that is known to be false. It cannot be said why the two bodies each move to the place of the other, and regarding circular motion, it is refuted by the motion of water in a vessel, for we know it is not.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative argument (burhan) in physics, specifically dealing with the nature of contact (tamās) and the impossibility of a void (khalā'). The author argues that if two surfaces separate instantly, a void must exist between them, which is metaphysically impossible. The argument proceeds in several steps: First, it establishes that when one surface contacts another, the contact occurs at points. Then it argues that if the surfaces separate at once, either they separate as surfaces or as points; if as points, overlapping occurs (absurd); if as surfaces, the void is created. The author then addresses the possibility of porous bodies, arguing that even with pores, there must be continuous surfaces, otherwise bodies would be composed of atoms. Finally, he discusses motion and transfer of bodies, showing that separation without a void leads to circularity or interpenetration. The examples used are from physical bodies and motions, typical of Islamic kalam and falsafa debates. The commentary follows the author's reasoning without importing outside information.

C) They move in a circular motion.
D) The bodies become atoms.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that instant surface separation necessarily produces a void because the only alternatives lead to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar reductio argument in Islamic kalam: Assume that atoms can separate instantly. Show that this would lead to either overlapping atoms or a void, both of which are impossible. Use the logical form from this lesson.
?

Refuting the Void with Conditional Syllogisms and Empirical Signs
Refutation of void with conditional syllogism and experiments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's conditional syllogism against the void and evaluate its validity using his empirical observations.

Core Idea
The void is impossible because it contradicts observed motion and the behavior of fluids.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi sets out to refute the existence of void (khala') using both logical demonstration and empirical observations. He constructs a conditional syllogism that shows the absurd consequences of void, then responds to objections by citing Avicenna and providing experimental evidence from water and air. This lesson walks you through the structure of his argument so you can see how Islamic philosophers combined reason and experiment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional syllogism
Find the 'if-then' structure: 'if the distance were void, then no collisions would occur, so the projectile's force would not weaken, so it would not return until reaching the celestial sphere.' This is the conditional argument.
The actual observed return of projectiles disproves the consequent, so the antecedent (void) must be false by modus tollens.
2
Examine the objection and response
An objector claims the argument only works if there is nothing between heaven and earth but void, but air is present. Al-Razi responds by citing Avicenna's Shifa to show that even in forced motion there is resistance and rest.
The objection challenges the premise that no collisions occur in void; al-Razi's response appeals to authority and deeper physics.
3
Analyze the empirical signs
Al-Razi describes experiments: a narrow tube with water, a siphon, and a flask. These show that water rises and flasks break to avoid void, indicating nature abhors a vacuum.
These are physical demonstrations that void is impossible in practice, supporting the logical argument.
4
Evaluate the overall argument
Combine the logical and empirical strands: the conditional syllogism proves necessity, and the experiments confirm that void cannot occur in the natural world.
Al-Razi's argument is a blend of demonstrative (burhan) and observational evidence, typical of Islamic natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال الخلاء. فلو كانت المسافة خالية لما حصلت المصادمات فكان يجب أن لا تضعف تلك القوة فكان يجب أن لا يرجع المحجر إلا بعد وصوله إلى سطح الفلك. ولقائل أن يقول: هذا الكلام لو قلنا ليس بين السماء والأرض إلا الخلاء فإن ذلك ينتقض بالبرهان. قلنا الغالب هو الهواء لكن من خلاله خلاء، لا يلزم ذلك. وأيضا فالشيخ ذكر في الشفاء في الموضع الذي بين فيه أن من الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة سكونا أن المتحرك القاسي كما يفيد القوة تحركه إلى فوق فانه بعيد قوة تكتم ذلك الموضع. وكان هذا قوله فكيف يمكنه مع ذلك أن يقول أنك لو لا مصادمات الهواء لما رجع الجو؟ النهج الثاني: العلامات. فأقالوا: الآن، التي قليل الضيق؟ إذا كان السفلة ثقبة ضيقة لف أعلى فان فتح دلسة نزل الماء و إن ضم ما نزل وليس ذلك إلا لاتقاء الخلاء. وكذا الأنبوبة إذا عمد أحد طرفيها إلى الماء و مص الطرف الآخر صعد الماء مع أنه ليس من شأن الماء الصعود؟ و ذلك لأن سطح الهواء ملائم لسطح الماء فاذا مص الهواء الجنب فتبعه الماء. وكذا الماء يرتفع عند المحجة. وكذلك إذا أدخلنا رأس أنبوبة داخل قارورة و أحكمنا الخلل الذي بين عنق القارورة وعنق الأنبوبة ليسد الخلل. فإن أدخلنا الأنبوبة والحالة هذه بحيث لا يدخل فيه الهواء انكسرت القارورة إلى داخل و ذلك لاستحالة الخلاء. و إن أدخلنا الأنبوبة إلى باطن القارورة أكثر انكسرت إلى خارج و ذلك لأن الآنية كانت خلوا فإذا أدخلنا فيها الماء عملها فانشقت إلى خارج. و لأنه لو أمكن الجيل أن يكون بعض المقادير خالية فإذا أنكرناها و مشاهدة الماء و اعتمدنا عليها وجب أن يصعد الماء إليها من غير أن يمتص منها هواء. و ذلك لا تظهر البقابيق و الفقاعات. و لقائل أن يقول: من الأحمد؟ فإن كانت توهم عند الخلاء الواحدة تمام بنت بالبرهان القاطع.
Refutation of the void. For if the distance were void, no collisions would occur, so that force would not weaken, so the projectile would not return except after reaching the celestial sphere. And an objector may say: This statement, if we say there is nothing between heaven and earth except void, then it is refuted by demonstration. We say: the prevalent is air, but through it there is void; that does not follow. Also, the Shaykh mentioned in the Shifa in the place where he showed that in ascending and descending motion there is rest, that the forced mover also provides the force to move upward, and it is distant from the force that conceals that place. This was his statement, so how can he also say that if it were not for collisions of air, the air would not return? The second approach: signs. They said: now, that little narrow? When the lower part is a narrow hole, twisted upward, then if you open a certain thing, water descends, and if you close it, what descended does not return, and that is only due to avoidance of void. Likewise, the tube: if you immerse one end in water and suck the other end, water rises, although it is not in the nature of water to ascend. That is because the surface of air adheres to the surface of water; so when you suck the air, the water follows. Likewise, water rises at the siphon. Similarly, if we insert the head of a tube inside a flask and seal the gap between the neck of the flask and the neck of the tube to block the gap. Then if we insert the tube in this state such that no air enters, the flask breaks inward due to the impossibility of void. And if we insert the tube further into the flask, it breaks outward, because the vessel was void, and when we introduced water, it forced it and split outward. And because if it were possible for some magnitudes to be void, then if we deny that, and observe water and rely on it, water would have to rise to it without sucking air from it. And that does not appear as bubbles and froths. And an objector may say: From Ahmad? For if it is imagined at the single void, it is perfectly refuted by the decisive demonstration.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the existence of void (khala'). The author presents a conditional syllogism: if the distance were void, then no collisions would occur, so the projectile's force would not weaken, and it would not return until reaching the celestial sphere—but we observe projectiles return, so void does not exist. Then an objection is raised: in a void, air is absent, but the argument depends on collisions. The author responds by citing Avicenna's Shifa where it is shown that even in forced motion, there is rest and resistance. He then provides empirical signs: water in a narrow tube, the siphon, and the flask experiment show that void is impossible because water and air follow to avoid void. The decisive proof is that if void existed, water would rise without suction, which does not happen. The passage demonstrates the use of demonstration (burhan) and empirical observation to refute a philosophical claim.

Key Terms

المصادمات
المحجر
الشفاء

D) The celestial sphere would shatter.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes void by combining a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) with empirical experiments showing that nature avoids vacuum.

?

Refuting the Claim That Separate Intellects Know All Things
A critical refutation of the claim that separate intellects know all things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the author's critique of the argument that a separate intellect must know all other separate forms.

Core Idea
Knowledge of self does not automatically yield knowledge of all other things.

This lesson walks through a sophisticated refutation from the Islamic critical tradition. The author dismantles the claim that a separate intellect (mujarrad) necessarily knows itself and every other separate form. You will see how he attacks the principles of uniformity, causality, and the nature of knowledge itself. This method is crucial for anyone studying Islamic theology (kalam) and its engagement with philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The opponent claims that a separate intellect, because it is abstract and self-subsisting, must know itself and all other separate forms. The author lists several sub-arguments supporting this claim. Locate these in the passage.
The claim is a cornerstone of Avicennan metaphysics.
2
Examine the first refutation: principle of uniformity
The author denies that the judgment of one thing applies to its like. He argues that two species sharing a genus may differ in essential attributes, so uniformity does not hold. Identify where he gives this counterexample.
Think of how this undermines arguments from analogy.
3
Analyse the refutation of self-knowledge implying knowledge of effects
The opponent argues that if an intellect knows itself as a cause, it must know its effects. The author refutes this by stating that knowledge of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect. Find this crucial step.
This is a key distinction between necessary and contingent relations.
4
Evaluate the final refutation: union of abstract form and intellect
The author challenges the claim that when an abstract form dwells in a rational substance, it becomes an intellect actually. He argues against union and shows the difficulties. Trace his reasoning on why the union fails.
This attacks the core mechanism of intellection in Peripatetic philosophy.

الامتناع ما جاء من جانب الجنس بل من جانب الفصل. انا نقول هذا ان الأمرين ذكرت لكني بذلك انه قديم على أحد المثلين والمنع على الآخر وذلك ما يختص بدنا تم ولزلنا ان المجرد اذا وجد خارجاً فتح ان يقارنه سائر الماهيات فلانة انه لازم من صحة ملك المقارنة كونه عاقلاً. فانا إلى الآن ما تا بنا لحدا منهم الشغري قامة الدلالة على ان العاقلية نفرة لك الحصول وانا لست عبارة عن اضافة زائدة عليه. ثم ولن حكمت الأم ذكرتم Similarly, we do not know that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. For the portion of each species from a genus shares the essence with the portion of the other species in the entirety of the essence, yet despite that, the essential attributes of each one are prevented from being with the other. Thus it is not possible that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. It cannot be said that this prevention came from the side of the genus rather than from the side of the differentia. We say: these are two matters mentioned, but by that I mean that it is old upon one of the two similars and prevention upon the other, and that pertains to our matter. And we have stated that if the abstract exists externally, then it is possible that it be accompanied by the rest of the essences, because it is necessary from the validity of the possibility of accompaniment that it is rational. For we have not yet found any of them who established the indication that rationality is a negation of possession, and I am not an expression of an additional relation upon it. Therefore every abstract knows its essence. Then if that abstract is a cause for itself, then it must know that other, because when it knows itself, it knows from itself that it is a cause for itself, and that necessitates its knowledge of the other. The objection: not everything that has the attributes of the abstract is present to it, for that is a relation that is only known upon difference, according to its determination. It cannot be said: you have occupied its essence, for you have related its essence to itself. We say: that is the opposite of worship. Then if that is the case, we do not know that everything whose essence is present to it knows it. What is the proof of the intellect affirming this presence? The pen of mercy is an expression of a specific relation, and it is obtained when an abstract essence is present to an abstract essence, and sometimes it is not obtained, for different essences are far from being different in that they are one. Then if we conceded that, we will repeat aspects that refute that. Then if you conceded that everything abstracts its essence, we do not concede that it must present its effects. Its explanation: knowledge of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect. The abstract form, when it dwells in the rational substance with a commanding command, is actually rational. And if that is the case, it is found that the abstract form is also actually intellect. As for the minor premise, the potential rational substance, when the intellectual form actually occurs to it, we indeed make the intellectual form actually an intellect. And from where is the desired? Or it remains distinguished from it in a state of indwelling in the body, and that is false, because the actual intellect is either from the place or from the attribute or from what, and the premise is false. So the claim of non-union is false. And we said that the intellect cannot be from the place, because there is no escape from the form dwelling in it not being intelligible to it, from the side of actualizing rationality, because everyone who knows something...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that a separate intellect (mujarrad) necessarily knows itself and all other separate forms. The author is engaging in a dialectical argument, likely against the peripatetic philosophers (like Ibn Sina). He breaks down the opponent's argument into several steps and refutes each. First, he denies that the judgment of one thing applies to its like (the principle of uniformity), using the example of two species sharing a genus but differing in essential attributes. He then addresses the claim that a separate intellect, because it is abstract (mujarrad), can be accompanied by other essences and thus must be rational (i.e., aware). He counters that rationality is not a mere negation or an additional relation, and that the opponent's proof is weak. Next, he questions why an intellect must know everything else, just because it knows itself. He rejects the claim that self-knowledge is self-evident, demanding a proof. He then refutes the idea that knowledge of self implies knowledge of being a cause, and hence knowledge of effects. He points out that the rule that 'knowing one thing implies knowing one's knowledge of it' does not hold universally, as it would entail that God (who is one) would have multiple knowledges, which is impossible. He also argues that an abstract essence's presence to itself does not automatically grant knowledge; it requires a relation that only obtains when there is difference. Finally, he attacks the notion that an abstract form dwelling in a rational substance becomes an intellect actually, arguing against union. The overall argument is complex and targets the necessitarian epistemology of the philosophers.

Why the Body Cannot Be the Source of Its Effects
The author refutes the view that the body itself is the efficient cause of its effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that the body is not the efficient cause of its own effects and apply it to prove the existence of a distinct power in Kalam.

Core Idea
The body's corporeality alone cannot explain its specific effects; a separate power is required.

This passage presents a critical argument against the view that the body itself is the source of its effects. The author systematically eliminates possibilities and uses the example of water to show that an inherent power distinct from the body must exist. This argument is foundational in Islamic Kalam for proving that the world requires a transcendent cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the disjunction
The author claims that any effect from a body requires an efficient cause (mu'aththir). He lists three possibilities: the body itself, a state of the body, or something separate. Recognize that this is a disjunctive syllogism.
The three possibilities are exhaustive for any effect residing in a body.
2
Examine the refutation of the first possibility
The author gives two arguments against the body being the cause: (1) All bodies are equal in corporeality but produce different effects, so corporeality cannot be the cause. (2) The effect can return (e.g., water becomes cold again) while the body remains, so the effect is not from the body. Understand why each argument refutes the claim.
The second argument is considered decisive by the author.
3
Analyze the elimination of the remaining possibilities
The second possibility (a state of the body) reduces to the first because the state is inseparable from the body. The third possibility (something separate) leads to infinite regress if the effect's specificity to certain bodies requires a separate cause for each. See how the author uses reductio ad absurdum.
This step shows the logical structure: if not A, then either B or C, but B and C also lead to contradictions.
4
Interpret the water example
Water heated by an external source returns to cold when the source is removed, indicating an inherent power (quwwa) to be cold. Unlike air, which retains heat, water's property shows a specific power. This example empirically supports the existence of a power distinct from the body.
The example contrasts water and air to demonstrate that different bodies have different inherent powers.
5
Conclude the proof
The only coherent conclusion is that the body is not the efficient cause; rather, a power (quwwa) inherent in the body but distinct from its corporeality is the source of the effect. This power is later identified as a faculty (malaka) in the soul.
This conclusion prepares for later discussions on qudra and malaka.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوة موجودة فيه ذلك الأثر من حادث فلابد له من مؤثر وهو إما الجسم المحل أو حاله أو ما لا يكون محلا له ولا حالا فيه. والاول باطل بوجهين: اما لأن تلك الأجسام بأسها متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة الآثار. وأما ثانيا فلأن الأثر قد يعود مع بقاء الجسمية، وبهذا الوجه خاصة بطل. القسم الثاني والثالث من المطلوب والبرهان باطل لأنه إن كان جسما أو جسمانيا عاد القسم الأول. وإن كان حصول أثره في بعض الأجسام دون البعض إن كان أثر اختص به ذلك الجسم لأجله استحق من ذلك المؤثر ذلك الأثر من المفارق في المطلوب. وإلا فقد شرح حدا في الأثر على الآف المرجح وأنه محال. والذي يبينه أنه كما أن الجسم مختص بالأثر الخاص فهو أيضا مختص بالقوة المؤثرة. فإن افتقر الاختصاص الأول إلى قوة فليفتقر الاختصاص الثاني أيضا إلى قوة أخرى لا إلى نهاية فهو محال. فإن الجسم بالاستعداد السابق كفى في ذلك فلم لا يكفي في الاختصاص؟ ولنأخذ مثالا: الماء إذا سُخن بقاسر فعند زوال القاسر يعود باردا. فلولا أن فيه قوة لمقتضى البرودة وإلا لكان عوده البرودة بسبب جديد مبرد. خلاف الهواء إذا أُسخن بقاسر فإن عند زوال القاسر لا يعود ذلك الهواء بارجا بل يبقى على طبيعة الهوائية. فعلمنا أن تلك القوة غير مستندة إلى قوة أخرى. الحلو جده أنه ملكة يصل بها عن النفس أفعال بسهولة من غير تقدم روية. والفرق بينه وبين أصل القدرة أن القدرة إلى الضدين على السواء بالوجه الذي عرفته وهو ليس كذلك. وليس عبادة عن تغير الفعل أيضا.
There is a power present in it that effect from an event, so it must have an efficient cause, and it is either the body that is the subject or its state, or something that is not a subject for it nor a state in it. The first is invalid for two reasons: firstly because those bodies as a whole are equal in corporeality and different in effects. And secondly because the effect may return with the persistence of corporeality, and by this aspect specifically it is invalid. The second and third divisions are invalid because if it were a body or corporeal, the first division returns. And if the occurrence of its effect in some bodies without others, if the effect is specific to that body because of which it deserves from that efficient cause that effect from the separate, then it is the required. And otherwise, he has explained a limit in the effect concerning the preferer [؟] and that it is impossible. And that which explains it is that just as the body is specific to the particular effect, it is also specific to the efficient power. So if the first specificity is in need of a power, then the second specificity is also in need of another power ad infinitum, which is impossible. For the body's prior disposition suffices for that, so why does it not suffice for the specificity? And let us take an example: water, when heated by a heater, when the heater is removed, it returns cold. For if there were no power in it requiring coldness, otherwise its return to coldness would be due to a new cooling cause. Unlike air, when heated by a heater, when the heater is removed, that air does not return cold but remains on its airy nature. So we know that that power is not dependent on another power. The [؟] he found that it is a faculty by which actions of the soul are performed easily without prior deliberation. And the difference between it and the original power is that the power is towards the two opposites equally in the manner you know, while this is not so. And it is not a habit of changing the action either.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument refuting the view that the body itself is the source of its effects. The author argues that any effect arising from a body must have an efficient cause (mu'aththir). He enumerates three possibilities: the cause is the body itself, a quality of the body, or something separate. He rejects the first possibility with two arguments: 1) All bodies are equal in their corporeality (jismiyya) yet produce different effects, so corporeality cannot be the cause of the specific effect. 2) The effect (like coldness in water) returns after being removed even though the body remains, which shows that the effect is not from the body itself. The second and third possibilities are also invalid because if the cause were a bodily quality, it would reduce to the first case (the body somehow causing it), and if it were something separate, then the specificity of effect to certain bodies would require an infinite regress of causes. He illustrates with the example of water: when heated by an external heater, upon removal of the heater, water returns to its original coldness. This shows that water has an inherent power (quwwa) to become cold again, not dependent on another external cause. This inherent power is a faculty (malaka) that allows the soul to act easily without prior reflection, and it differs from the original power (qudra) which is equally disposed to opposites. The author concludes that this faculty is not a mere habit of changing actions.

ملكة

C) Because water is naturally cold, so no power is needed to explain its coldness.
D) Because water can be heated and cooled repeatedly, showing it has no fixed power.

Key Takeaway
The body is not the efficient cause of its effects; a distinct inherent power accounts for specific effects, as shown by the water example and the logical elimination of other possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting Objections to the Proof of the Creator: A Dialectical Analysis
Dialectical refutation of four objections against divine creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four objections against the proof of a creator and reconstruct the author's responses using the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
A valid theological proof withstands objections by addressing each on its own logical grounds.

In this dialectical passage, the author engages with hypothetical objectors who challenge the proof of a creator and the temporal origination of the world. You will learn how to map objections and responses, which is essential for structured theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim being defended
The overall thesis is that the world is temporally originated and requires a creator. This is the position the author defends against all objections.
2
Locate the first objection and response
The objector argues that if effects are preponderated without a differentiator, a necessitating cause suffices. The author responds that such preponderance implies a preponderating factor, which is the creator.
The objection is partly reconstructed due to OCR issues.
3
Find the second objection (time and will) and response
The objector questions why the world was created at a specific time. The author argues that if time is considered, the will depends on it; if not, the world would be perpetual. Thus God's will is independent of time.
4
Identify the third objection (knowledge and vicious circle) and response
The objector claims that knowledge of occurrence follows the event. The author points out that making continuation depend on knowledge would cause a vicious circle.
5
Locate the fourth objection (benefits/maṣlaḥa) and response
The objector argues that divine action must consider benefit. The author counters that if benefit were necessary, creating the disbeliever (who has no benefit) would not occur – but it does. Hence benefit is not a necessary condition.

الذي هو تبع الايقاع فلو جعلنا الابقاء تبع العلم بالوقوع فيه لزم الدور وعن الرابع فاعلية البارى تعالى و تقدس ان اعتبر فيها دعاية المصلحة وجب فيما لا تكون مصلحة الا يوجد لكن خلق الكافر وتكليفه مع علمه كونه ياما سائر الاوقات في مجرد كونه وقتا يستدعى مخصصا وحينئذ يعود الاشكال واما نعلم بالصورة ان الله تعالى لو قدم خلق العالم على الوقت الذي خلفه فيه بجزء من الالف من الجنة واحدة أو زاد فيه جزء الا تجري تعالى كونه مسبوقًا بالعدم حتى يلزم المحال الذي ذكر مقة ولكننا ذلك لكنه لو وقع العالم قبل الوقت الذي وقع فيه المقدار سنة فقط لا يخل بذلك شيء من مصالح المكلفين بل يلزم تحقق الاشكال الفاق هذا السوال باطل لانه عاد ما كان تقديرا انا ندفع بل هو سوال
The attribute of worthiness and the power that subsists in the bearer of that worthiness likewise. So either the effect of that power in that body is a delayed origination, and the body that is spherical in shape — then the limbs of animals ought to be in the form of spheres joined together from behind, or its advantage therein is similar, while its relation to all parts of its bearer is equal. For you have allowed in the necessitating cause that some of its effects are preponderated over others while its relation to all is equal. And if the type of necessitating cause is such that it is more fitting in the powerful, then that would be preferable. But if the second [is the case], then the Bestower of forms has specified each of the materials of the limbs with a particular form, while the relation of all those materials to all those forms is equal. That detracts from the principle of the world's states, for it is eternal in essence but originated in attributes. And by your statement '134 in the pages and you forced upon us the origination of the world's essence according to the principles established in the beginnings of intellects: that Allah, whose relation to existence is like the relation of the foot to non-existence without any preponderance of one of the two sides over the other — for it is impossible to preponderate one of the two alternatives except by a separate matter. And if the proposition is evident in its universality, it includes the powerful and the necessitating other. And if it is not inclusive in some of our particulars, then that is an explanation of the whole, so we should [proceed] by the chapter of proving the Creator. And regarding the second: that the obligation of the attachment of Allah's will to the effect of the world at a specific time: either that specific time is considered, so the first object is dependent on it, and its refutation will come; or it is not considered, so then the will of the creator is attached to bringing about the world without the presence of that time having any effect, and then it follows that the world occurs whether that time is present or not, and then the world becomes perpetual. And regarding the third: that knowledge of occurrence is at a time, but it follows the occurrence at that time which follows the bringing about; so if we made the continuation follow knowledge of occurrence, a vicious circle would result. And regarding the fourth: the agency of the Supreme and Exalted, if consideration of benefit is taken into account in it, then it is necessary that what has no benefit does not exist; but the creation of the disbeliever and his obligation while knowing that he deserves only punishment has no benefit, so it must not exist — this is a contradiction. And if consideration of those benefits is not taken into account, then the basis of the answer is invalidated; for we do not conceive a causation in His agency by observing benefit. But that the specific time is the source of benefit while it is like other times in merely being a time requires a distinguisher, and then the problem returns. And we know by the form that if Allah had created the world before the time at which He created it by a part of a thousandth, or increased it by a part, it would not affect anything of the benefits of the responsible beings, as you have questioned upon it. And regarding the temporal limits: the effect of powers in something does not require the effect to be preceded by non-existence, as I have previously stated. So it does not follow from the world's occurrence now from Allah that it was preceded by non-existence such that the impossible mentioned follows. But we have that, but if the world had occurred before the time at which it occurred by only one year, that would not affect anything of the benefits of the responsible beings; rather, the problem would be realized. This question is invalid because it is a return of what was originally a estimation we refute; rather it is a question.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author refutes objections against the proof of a creator and the temporal origination of the world. It begins by arguing that the preponderance of some effects over others without a differentiator implies a preponderating factor, which in the case of the world points to a creator (the Bestower of forms). The author then addresses four specific objections: (1) The objection regarding the necessity of a specific time for the world's creation is refuted by showing that if time is not considered, the world would be perpetual, contradicting its temporal nature. (2) The objection about knowledge and occurrence is answered by pointing out that making continuation dependent on knowledge of occurrence leads to a vicious circle. (3) The objection concerning benefits in divine action is refuted by stating that if benefits are not considered, the original answer fails; if they are considered, the creation of disbelievers with no benefit would be impossible, which is a contradiction. Therefore, benefits are not a factor. (4) The objection about the effect not requiring temporal precedence is answered by affirming that the world's occurrence now does not necessitate prior non-existence; the problem is only an estimation, not a valid objection.

الاستحقاق
المصلحة

A) God's actions are above benefit, so the objection is irrelevant.
B) The creation of disbelievers shows that benefit is not a necessary condition for divine action.
C) Benefit is always considered but hidden from human understanding.
D) The objection is based on a false assumption about time.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes four objections by showing that each either leads to a contradiction or is based on a mistaken assumption, thereby defending the proof of a creator.

The Compound Argument for the Creator: A Logical Reconstruction
Multi-premise argument for the creator's existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the multi-step argument that the author uses to defend the proof of a creator, identifying the intermediate conclusions and how they support the final claim.

Core Idea
A compound argument weaves multiple premises together to establish a conclusion that no single syllogism can reach.

While the previous lesson examined the dialectical structure, this lesson focuses on the underlying logical skeleton. The author's argument is not a single syllogism but a chain of reasoning involving preponderance, will, knowledge, and the nature of divine action. You will learn to formalise such compound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conclusion
The overall thesis is that the world requires a creator who is powerful and willing. This is the claim being supported by multiple sub-arguments.
2
Extract the first sub-argument: preponderance
The author argues that the preponderance of some effects over others without a differentiator implies a preponderating factor, which is the creator. This is a reasoning from observed diversity to a necessary cause.
This sub-argument is a form of argument from design.
3
Extract the second sub-argument: time and will
The world's occurrence at a specific time requires a will that is not dependent on time; otherwise the world would be perpetual. This shows that the creator's will is eternal and free.
4
Extract the third sub-argument: knowledge and occurrence
Knowledge of occurrence follows the actual occurrence, not the reverse. Making continuation depend on knowledge would cause a vicious circle, so the creator's knowledge is not the basis of the world's continuation.
5
Extract the fourth sub-argument: benefit (maṣlaḥa)
Divine action does not require consideration of benefit. If it did, the creation of disbelievers (who have no benefit) would be impossible, but it occurs. Therefore, benefit is not a necessary condition.
