Refuting Eternity via Intermediate Intellect
Author proposes a counter-hypothesis to undermine the proof of the eternity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's counter-hypothesis and identify how it undermines the proof of the eternity of bodies.

Core Idea
A single counterexample can dismantle a proof of eternity without disproving each premise.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical refutation of the philosophers' argument for the eternity of bodies. Rather than attacking the premises directly, he proposes a plausible alternative scenario that shows the conclusion does not follow. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of that counter-hypothesis and the ensuing objection-response exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The philosophers claim that bodies are eternal. The author grants some premises but then offers a counter-hypothesis to show the argument fails.
The 'count' refers to the structure of the philosophers' argument.
2
Reconstruct the counter-hypothesis
The author proposes that the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) is the cause of an eternal intellect or soul, which has partial perceptions and wills. These wills eventually lead to the creation of bodies, so bodies are originated (haditha), not eternal.
This avoids attributing temporal wills directly to God.
3
Analyze the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that partial intentions require partial perceptions, and partial perceptions need bodily organs. Since no bodily organs exist in the intermediate intellect, the hypothesis fails.
The opponent assumes perception is always through physical means.
4
Evaluate the author's response
The author denies that partial perceptions necessarily require bodily organs, citing future proof from psychology. Thus the objection is not decisive.
This keeps the counter-hypothesis viable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ولئن سلنا صحة ذلك العدد لكنه يمكنهم مع القول بذلك العدد إثبات قدم الأجسام؟ الاختمار أن يقال: إن واجب الوجود مريد بإرادة حادثة [؟] وكل سابق منها علة لحصول اللاحق على الوجه الذي ذكر من الحركات ثم إن تلك الإرادات [؟] تحتاج إلى إرادة متعلقة بإيجاد الأجسام مع أحد الحوادث [؟] وإن كانت لها بداية إلا أن الأجسام مع ذلك تكون حادثة [؟] هذا النمط لو صح كون البارئ تعالى عالماً بجميع [؟] قاصداً إلى إيجادها لكن ذلك محال: أما أولاً فلأنه غير عالم بجزئيات [؟] وأما ثانياً فلأنه تعالى يجل أن يكون قاصداً إلى إيجاد هذه الأشياء [؟] ثم ولئن سلمنا ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: ذات واجب الوجود علة لذاته لوجود موجود غير جسماني كعقل أو نفس وذلك الموجود له إدراكات جزئية وإرادات جزئية لا بداية لها ثم إنه انتهت تلك الإرادات إلى إرادة متعلقة بخلق الأجسام وعلى هذا الوجه تكون الأجسام محدثة؟ فإن قالوا: القصود الجزئية لا تصل إلا مع الإدراكات الجزئية [؟] ومتى لا تحصل تلك إلا بالآلات الجسمانية فيلزم من لا [؟] قدم تلك [؟] لا قدم الجسم [؟] ولن نسلم: أن الإدراكات الجزئية للإنسان لا تكون إلا بالآلات الجسمانية على ما سيأتي تفيد في علم النفس. وإذا كان كذلك كان الاحتمال الذي ذكرناه قائماً؛ ومع قيامه لا يمكن الجزم بقدم الأجسام.
Then, if we grant the correctness of that count, still they can, while saying that count, prove the eternity of bodies? The conjecture is to say: The Necessary of Existence is willing with a temporal will [?], and every preceding [will] is a cause for the occurrence of the succeeding in the manner mentioned regarding motions. Then those wills [?] need a will related to bringing bodies into existence along with one of the events [?], and even if they have a beginning, still the bodies would be originated? This pattern, if it were correct that the Creator is omniscient and intends to bring them into existence, but that is impossible: first because He is not knowledgeable of particulars [?], second because He is too exalted to intend to bring these things into existence [?]. Then, if we grant that, why is it not permissible to say: The essence of the Necessary of Existence is a cause for itself for the existence of a non-bodily being, like an intellect or soul, and that being has partial perceptions and partial wills without beginning, then those wills conclude with a will related to the creation of bodies; and in this manner the bodies are originated? If they say: Partial intentions cannot reach except with partial perceptions, and those are not obtained except through bodily organs, so it follows from the non-eternity of those [?] the non-eternity of the body? We do not grant that partial human perceptions occur only through bodily organs, as will come in psychology. And if so, the probability we mentioned stands; and with it standing, one cannot be certain of the eternity of bodies.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a proof for the eternity of bodies (qidam al-ajsam). The author grants a premise (the count of arguments) but then offers a counter-hypothesis that undermines the conclusion. The opponent argues that the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) must have a temporal will to create bodies, but the author points out that this leads to impossibilities: God cannot be the subject of temporal wills, nor can He have partial knowledge. The author then proposes a different model: the Necessary Existent's essence is the cause of an eternal intellect or soul, which itself has eternal partial perceptions and wills, and eventually wills the creation of bodies. This alternative shows that bodies could be originated (haditha) even if the world is not eternal in the philosophers' sense. The opponent objects that such partial intentions require bodily organs, but the author refutes this by asserting that human partial perceptions do not necessarily require bodily organs—a claim to be proven in psychology. Thus, the hypothesis stands, and the proof of eternity fails. The argument follows a dialectical pattern: objection (naqd) and response (jawab), characteristic of jadal.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud): The Necessary Existent, whose existence is necessary in itself and not contingent. [Note: Stable across the tradition; used in falsafah and kalam but with different connotations (kalam emphasizes will).]
إرادة حادثة (irada haditha): A temporal or originated will; the author argues it is impossible for the Necessary Existent. [Note: Key concept in the debate on God's attributes; for philosophers, God's will is eternal, for theologians it is often temporal.]
عقل (aql): An intellect, a separate substance that is non-bodily and can have eternal perceptions. [Note: In falsafah, the active intellect is a mediator; in kalam, it may be rejected.]
الآلات الجسمانية (al-alat al-jismaniyya): Bodily organs or instruments required for sense perception; the author denies they are necessary for all partial perceptions. [Note: Stable; debated in psychology whether the soul can perceive without body.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's counter-hypothesis, what mediates the creation of bodies from the Necessary Existent?
A) A temporal will directly from the Necessary Existent.
B) An eternal intellect or soul with partial perceptions and wills.
C) A set of physical causes inherited from earlier motions.
D) The essence of the Necessary Existent directly without any intermediary.

Key Takeaway
The author's counter-hypothesis demonstrates that a proof of eternity is not conclusive if a plausible alternative scenario of origination exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a counter-hypothesis to the following claim: 'The world is eternal because every change requires a prior cause, and the chain of causes cannot regress infinitely.' Your counter-hypothesis should introduce an intermediate entity that allows for an origin of the world without violating the principle of causality.
?
In a debate with a philosopher who argues that the soul is eternal because it is simple and indestructible, formulate an objection (mu'arada) similar to the author's style, proposing an alternative explanation for the soul's nature.

The Possible Always Needs a Cause: Persistence Proof
Author argues that the possible requires a cause both in origination and persistence, refuting the view that the effect becomes independent once existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that a possible being requires a cause in persistence, and identify the role of the priority (awlawiyya).

Core Idea
The possible's need for a cause does not end at creation; it persists because the priority of existence itself is contingent.

In Islamic theology (kalam), the distinction between origination and persistence is crucial. Some philosophers argued that once a possible being exists, it no longer needs a cause. Al-Razi's refutation in this passage shows that the possible always depends on a cause, even during persistence, because the very appropriateness of existence over non-existence is contingent. This lesson breaks down his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the claim being proved
The author aims to prove that a possible (mumkin) requires a cause not only for its origination but also for its persistence (baqa').
The opposite view, held by some philosophers, is that once an effect exists, it becomes self-sufficient.
2
2. Extract the key concept: priority (awlawiyya)
At persistence, existence has a 'priority' or 'appropriateness' over non-existence. This priority is critical to the argument.
Awlawiyya means that existence is 'more worthy' than non-existence at that state.
3
3. Analyze the two-case dilemma on the origin of this priority
The priority either existed before the state of persistence or it arises anew. Each case is examined separately.
This is a classic disjunctive syllogism (either A or B).
4
4. For each case, show why the effect remains dependent
If the priority existed before and was sufficient to make the effect independent, then the effect would have been independent at origination (contradiction). If insufficient, dependence remains. If the priority is new, it itself requires a cause, so the effect depends on that cause.
In all scenarios, the effect does not become independent of the cause.
5
5. Understand the rebuttal about relationality (idafa)
The need for God is a relation that only exists when the two relata (God and the effect) are realized. If the effect's persistence depended on an eternal cause, that cause would have to be simultaneous, which is impossible for a temporal being.
This counters the objection that temporally originated things are possible at all times but only exist at one time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أنا مع ذلك ننكر وجوهاً [؟] بلية في هذه المسئلة. و الراشد المستمر حال استمر له [؟] مكن، والمكن تحتاج إلى السبب، فأمر انت حالاً شماره [؟] محتاج إلى السبب. فالمحتاج إلى السبب لايجب أن يكون حادثاً لاتصال [؟] المكن محتاج في وجوده إلى السبب حالَ [؟] الايكون الوجود أولى به من العدم. أما حال البقا فالوجود أولى به من العدم، فلا جرم يستغني عن المؤثر، لأن القواد [؟] هذه الأولوية: إما أن يقال أنها كانت حاصلة قبل زمان البقاء أو ما كانت حاصلة. فإن كان الأول: فإن كانت مغنية عن المؤثر لزم استغناء المكن حال حدوثه عن السبب، هذا خلف. وإن لم تكن مغنية لزم أن لا يستغني الأثر حال بقائه عن السبب. وإن كان الثاني: فتلك الأولوية حادثة فلابد لها من سبب، فالمعلول يفتقر إلى تلك الأولوية حال البقاء، فيكون المكن حال بقائه مفتقراً إلى السبب. وصدوده عن الله [؟] نع [؟] وقائى الله تعالى فيه وجمع ما توقف عليه هذه الأشهر [؟] إن لم يكن مكناً لذاته في كل وقت لم يكن ممكناً في وقت، وإلا لزم انقلاب الممكن لذاته ممتنعاً لذاته، هذا خلف. وإن كان ممكناً في كل وقت فقد تحقق صدود الأثر [؟] الآن للسائل أن يُعارِض ذلك بالحادث الزماني من حيث [؟] سَوْكَن [؟] كل فان [؟] أي مربوط ببق [؟] العدم. صحة وجوده حاصلة في وقت صحت [؟] حضر [؟] لها في كل وقت ثم لم يلزم من [؟] وجوب [؟] متحدة [؟] حصولها في الآن لما بينه وبين الأزلية من المنافاة. الحاجة إلى الله أمر إضافي، والإضافات لا تتحقق إلا عند تحقق المضافين. فلو كان وجود الفعل أو تأثير الفاعل فيه [؟] حفيقاً [؟] إلى القدم المتقدم لزم أن تكون القدم المتقدم مقارناً هذا خلف في أن الوجود و حلم [؟] الصلح [؟] للمعلية [؟] ولا للمحلولة [؟] صفة من ذهب إلى أنه لا قاثر [؟] للعلة إلا الموجود الذي للمعلول [؟] أحلى [؟] ما [؟] حقياتها [؟] فلا. ومنهم من جعل وجود الله الذي [؟] يمونه [؟] بالعقل الأول علة لما بعده من العقول، وذلك باطل لحدوثه.
Then we, despite that, deny [some] problematic aspects in this issue. The [object] that persists continuously in its state of persistence is a possible [being], and the possible needs a cause. So the [object] that is in one state [of existence] is in need of a cause. The one in need of a cause does not have to be temporally originated because the possible is constantly in need of a cause in its existence at the state where existence is more appropriate than non-existence. As for the state of persistence, existence is more appropriate than non-existence, so it becomes independent of the agent, because the priority of this appropriateness: either it is said that it occurred before the time of persistence or it did not occur. If the first: if it is sufficient for the agent, then the possible would be independent at the time of its origination from the cause, which is a contradiction. If it is not sufficient, then it follows that the effect is not independent at the time of its persistence from the cause. If the second: then that priority is temporally originated, so it must have a cause, so the effect depends on that priority at the time of persistence, so the possible is in need of the cause at the time of its persistence. And its derivation from God? ... If it is not a possible in essence at every time, it would not be possible at any time, otherwise it would lead to the conversion of the possible in essence to the impossible in essence, which is a contradiction. And if it is possible at every time, then the derivation of the effect has been actualized now. The questioner may object to that with the temporal originated thing from the perspective that every mortal [thing] is connected to the persistence of non-existence. The soundness of its existence occurred at a time; it would have been correct for it to occur at every time, yet it did not necessitate a single [thing] to occur in the moment because of the incompatibility between it and eternity. The need for God is an added relation, and added relations are not realized except when the two relata are realized. So if the existence of the act or the effect of the agent upon it were dependent on the preexistent eternity, it would necessitate that the preexistent eternity be simultaneous, which is a contradiction regarding existence and [the notion of] suitability for causality and for [the effect's] attribute. Some went to the view that there is no effect of the cause except the existence that belongs to the effect, [claiming] that the basis of its realities is not [something else]. And some of them made the existence of God, which He [?] with the First Intellect, a cause for what comes after it of intellects, and that is false because of its temporal origination.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the claim that a possible being (mumkin) requires a cause both for its origination and for its persistence. The author begins by stating that what persists continuously is a possible being, and every possible being needs a cause. He then distinguishes two states: the state of origination and the state of persistence. At origination, the possible needs a cause because existence is not intrinsic to it. At persistence, existence is more appropriate than non-existence, which might suggest independence, but the author argues that this appropriateness itself must be examined. The key argument: The priority (al-awlawiyya) of existence over non-existence at persistence either existed before persistence or not. If it existed before, then if it was sufficient to make the effect independent, the effect would have been independent at origination, which is false; if it was not sufficient, then the effect is still dependent. If the priority did not exist before, then it is temporally originated and needs a cause, making the effect dependent on that cause. Thus, the possible always needs a cause. The objection (naqd) concerning temporally originated things argues that they are possible at every time but only exist at one specific time due to incompatibility with eternity. The response (jawab) explains that the need for God is a relation (idafa) that only arises when the two relata exist; thus, if the effect's existence were dependent on the preexistent eternity, that would require the preexistent to be simultaneous, which is impossible. The author then refutes the views of those who say the cause only produces the effect's existence but not its persistence, and those who posit a chain of intellects.

أولوية
إضافي

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the analysis of priority (awlawiyya) at persistence show?
A) That priority makes the effect independent of the cause.
B) That priority either existed before or arises new, and in both cases the effect depends on a cause.
C) That the possible can become necessary if the priority is eternal.
D) That persistence is identical to origination.

Key Takeaway
The possible's need for a cause is perpetual; the contingency of the priority of existence ensures its ongoing dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Construct the main syllogism from the passage in Barbara form, using an example from fiqh: Every act of worship requires intention (niyya). Prayer is an act of worship. Therefore prayer requires intention. Identify the middle term and verify the figure and mood.
?

The Effect's Dependence on a Cause for Persistence
Author argues that an effect requires a cause for its continued existence, not just origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's argument that an effect needs a cause for its persistence and identify his refutations of alternative views.

Core Idea
The priority of existence over non-existence in persistence itself demands a cause, otherwise the effect would be independent at origination.

In this passage, al-Razi takes on the claim that once an effect originates, it no longer needs a cause to continue existing. He dissects this view using a careful logical dilemma and then refutes several alternative explanations—including the notion that possibility (imkan) is a cause or that temporal origination alone suffices. Understanding his argument sharpens our grasp of the metaphysical principle that every contingent being depends on a sustaining cause at every moment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Locate the position al-Razi is criticizing: that an effect, once originated, does not require a cause for its continued existence.
The claim is that in the state of persistence (ḥāl al-baqāʾ), the effect can do without a cause.
2
Follow the dilemma on priority
Al-Razi sets up a dilemma about the 'priority of existence over non-existence' in the state of persistence. Was this priority present before persistence or not?
If it was present and sufficient, the effect would have dispensed with the cause even at origination (false). If insufficient, the effect still depends. If the priority arises anew, it needs a cause itself, leading to a regress.
3
Examine the objection from temporal origination
Al-Razi considers an objection: that the need for a cause is a relation that only arises when the effect exists, so persistence doesn't require a new cause.
He refutes this by arguing that if the cause is eternal, the relation would require the effect to be eternal too, which is impossible for a temporal originated thing.
4
Analyze the refutations of alternative causal theories
Al-Razi criticizes three specific views: (1) the First Intellect as cause of subsequent intellects, (2) possibility (imkan) as a cause, and (3) an analogy about accidental inherence.
Each refutation shows that the supposed cause either leads to absurd consequences or is itself dependent on a prior cause.

Non‑existence in the state of persistence: existence is more appropriate for it than non‑existence, so it necessarily dispenses with the cause, because the priority of existence over non‑existence: either it is said that this priority was present before the state of persistence, or it was not present. If the first, then if it sufficed for the cause, the effect would have dispensed with the cause in the state of origination, which is false; and if it did not suffice, then the effect would not dispense with the cause in the state of persistence. If the second, then the priority is true, so it must have a cause; thus the effect in persistence depends on that priority, and that priority depends on a new cause, so the effect in its persistence depends on a new cause. ... And the objector may oppose that by the temporal originated in that its every part is connected to preceding non‑existence; the validity of its existence is obtained at a time, and if validity were present at every time, then it would not fail to occur, but it occurs only at that moment because of the incompatibility between it and eternity. The need for God is a relation, and relations are only realized when the two relata are realized. If the existence of the act or the effect of the agent were dependent on prior eternity, then the prior eternity would have to be contemporary, which is a violation... And there are those who make the existence of God, which is emanated through the First Intellect, a cause for what follows from the intellects, and that is false because of its origination. The existence of every cause and every effect includes the existence of another, and causes are essentially equal in rulings, so that every cause would produce what every other cause produces. If the effect were one in existence, or if one thing were a cause in that it is the cause of the red, then it would be necessary that all possibilities be equal... And as for the claim that possibility is a non‑existent state, and non‑existent states cannot be causes for existence... And also the argument about the inherence of accident in a substrate: it is said that its inherence in the substrate either is through participation of that substrate, which is impossible, or not through its participation, so the inherence would be dispensed with the substrate, thus the inherenter would be independent of it, and since existence is part of the inherence, the inherenter would be independent... And as this speech is weak.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of causality, specifically the claim that an effect does not require a cause for its continued existence. The author first examines the argument that existence is more appropriate than non‑existence in the state of persistence, so the effect should dispense with a cause. He breaks it into two possibilities: either the priority of existence over non‑existence was present before persistence or not. If it was present and sufficient, then the effect would have dispensed with the cause even at origination, which is false. If it was present and insufficient, then the effect still needs a cause in persistence. If the priority was not present before, then it arises anew and itself requires a cause, leading to a regress. Thus the effect is always dependent. The author then considers an objection from temporal originated things: their existence is only possible at a specific moment because of incompatibility with eternity, so need for a cause is a relation that arises only when the effect exists. He refutes this by arguing that relations require both relata, so if the cause is eternal, the effect would also have to be eternal. He then criticizes the view that the First Intellect is the cause of subsequent intellects, and the view that possibility (imkan) is a non‑existent state and thus cannot be a cause. Finally, he attacks an analogy about inherence of accidents, showing that it leads to absurd consequences. The entire passage is a dense critique of various attempts to deny the need for a sustaining cause.

In al-Razi's dilemma, what happens if the priority of existence over non-existence in the state of persistence was present before persistence and sufficed for the cause?
A) The effect would have dispensed with the cause in the state of origination, which is false.
B) The effect would still need a cause in persistence because the priority is insufficient.
C) The priority itself would need a new cause, leading to a regress.
D) The effect would be eternal and independent of any cause.

Key Takeaway
Every contingent being depends on a sustaining cause at every moment of its existence; the priority of existence over non-existence in persistence itself demands a cause.

?

Natural Bodily Force Cannot Produce Infinite Motion
Demonstration that natural force is finite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the demonstration that a natural bodily force cannot produce infinite motion by analyzing the ratio of force to subject size.

Core Idea
A finite bodily force cannot produce infinite effects because its parts are finite and the whole is only proportional.

In this passage, the author argues that natural force, being divided with its subject, cannot produce infinite motion. He uses a compound argument: if a part of the force produces finite motion, the whole must be finite by proportion; if a part produces infinite motion, the whole either fails to exceed it (absurd) or exceeds it (impossible). This reasoning is fundamental to Islamic natural philosophy, where the finitude of physical forces is linked to the temporality of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key premise about natural force
The author states that every bodily force is divided with the division of its subject, so the force of the whole is stronger than the force of a part. Write down this premise in your own words.
2
Analyze the first case: smaller force produces finite motion
If the smaller force moves the body a finite number of motions, then by the ratio of forces (part to whole), the whole force must also produce a finite number of motions. Express this as a proportion.
The ratio of effects equals the ratio of forces.
3
Analyze the second case: smaller force produces infinite motion
If the smaller force produces infinite motions, then the whole force either does not exceed it (which is impossible because the whole is stronger) or exceeds it (which would add an infinite increase to infinite motions, also impossible).
Both possibilities lead to contradiction.
4
Conclude that natural force cannot produce infinite motion
Since both possible outcomes (finite or infinite for the smaller force) lead to the conclusion that the whole force is finite, the demonstration is complete. State the conclusion in one sentence.
The author's argument is a dilemma.

عن تلك القوة فاذن الجان لو ا خلفان ذلك القبول لا لكن ذلك الاخلات بسبب المنيك بر سبب المحرك فان القوة في الحجر الاكبر اعظم حملة الاصغر لان الموجودة الأصغى موجود ع الأكبرون ياده واما القوة المشرقة فانہ مختلف تحر كما للجم العظيم والصغير الإخلاف المحرك بل ان القابل كلما كان اعظم كان العالق فيه الكبر واذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فقوك اما الفقة الطبيعية فانه طبع فيها أن تحرك تحريكا غير متناه لان كل قوة جمانية فهي منقمة بالقسام محلها ففقة الكل اقوى من قوة للبعض الو الفرد فاذا حل كتا جميعهما من مبداء مفروض فان حركت الفقة الصغرى حركات غير متناهية فالكبرى ان لم تزد عليها كان حال الله لا مع غير مكحاله مع غيره واذا راحت عليها وقعت الزيادة ع اغير المتناهي من الحمية التي مو بها غير متناه وان حركت الصغرى حركات متناهية وقد ثبت ان نية الأثرين كنية المؤثرين ونسبة بعض القوة الى كلها نسبة شاء الى قناه كانت نسبة المكتين ايضا نسبة متناه الى مناه ففعل كل القوة متناه وهو المطلوب واما القوة القسرية فيستحيل ان يكون فعلها غير متناه لان تحريكها لكل الجسم من بد امعين اقل من تحريكها جزءا منه منفع زيادة حركة الجن عا حركة الكل من الجانب الذى فرض الكل غير متناه فيه وذلك ايضا محاله بم ولكامل للقوات هذا ينقض ما اصلكم بات دورات العمر اكثر من دورات تحل مع ان ذلك لم يقيض ما قولكم اولاً لها فل ثم عند باتِ الحكم عليه بالن بادة والنقصان
So regarding what they mentioned that the bodily force only affects through sharing of position, the meaning of this statement is that the bodily force does not show effect except in its place or in what is adjacent to its place, and in what is adjacent to that adjacent, and its effect on what is closer to its place is prior to its effect on what is farther. And when the desired result is obtained, reliance in its proof is on experience. And what is said: its effect on what is near its place, if it were like its effect on what is far from it, it would not be more fitting for it to be a state in that place than for it to be a state in another. [This is] weak and denied. The logic: you say that the bodily force is not capable of what is infinite at all. If that were true, motion would only occur in time, which is impossible. And every motion in a divisible distance — half of it is before half of it, then all of it in time, and that is also impossible. For if it traverses half the distance in half that time, then its traversal of all of it would be faster than its traversal of all of it, so its traversal of all of it would not be the fastest of motions. And one may say: why do you say that the possibility which we considered is possible in itself — that the bodily force is not capable of what is infinite in terms of number and duration? And to proceed, we present a premise: that force is either natural or coercive. As for natural force, it must be that the greater body receives from the mover that which is like what the smaller receives; otherwise the obstacle is either the nature [of the body] or its concomitants, and this is manifestly false, or something beyond that, which is either natural — and that is impossible — or coercive; and the obstacle to natural motion is natural or coercive, and we have assumed it absent. So it becomes clear that the great and small body cannot differ in receiving motion from that force. Therefore, if they differed in that reception, that difference is due to the mover, not due to the receiver. For the force in the larger stone is greater than that of the smaller, because the power existing in the smaller exists in the larger and more. As for the coercive force, its effect varies for the great and small body not due to the mover's difference, but rather the receiver, whenever it is greater, the resistance in it is greater. And if you know this premise, then we say: as for natural force, it is in its nature that it moves with an infinite movement, because every bodily force is divided by the division of its subject, so the force of the whole is stronger than the force of some of the individual parts. So if both move from a supposed starting point, and if the smaller force moves infinite movements, then the larger force, if it does not exceed it, its state [would be] nothing relative to the other; but if it does exceed it, then the increase would be upon the infinite from the motions that are themselves infinite. And if the smaller force moves finite movements, and it has been established that the ratio of the two effects is as the ratio of the two agents, and the ratio of some force to all of it is as the ratio of a part to its whole, then the ratio of the two effects is also as finite to infinite; so the effect of all force is finite, which is what is sought. And as for coercive force, it is impossible that its effect be infinite, because its moving of the whole body from a given point is less than its moving of a part of it from that same point, with an increase of the motion of the whole over the motion of the part from the side assumed to be infinite, and that is also impossible. And for the completion of the forces, this refutes your principle that the rotations of the celestial sphere are more than the rotations of the sun, but that does not refute our statement that first they are... then at that point you judge them to be increasing and decreasing.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a compound argument from the physics section of logic, likely dealing with the demonstration of the finitude of bodily forces. The author begins by clarifying a claim that bodily force only affects things sharing its position, meaning it only acts in its immediate place or adjacent areas, with nearer effects being prior. He notes that this is ultimately established by experience. He then refutes a weak objection that if the force acted equally on near and far, then its being a state in one place would be no more fitting than another. The core argument proceeds: bodily force cannot produce infinite effects. If it could, motion would occur without time, which is impossible. Every motion across a divisible distance occupies time, and half the distance is traversed in half the time; if the whole distance were traversed in the same time as half, that would entail a contradiction. An objector then raises the possibility that the force might be incapable of infinite things in number and duration. The author responds by first distinguishing force into natural and coercive. For natural force, the larger body must receive motion proportional to its size; otherwise the obstacle would be either natural (impossible) or coercive (assumed absent), so the difference in reception must be due to the mover, not the receiver. Indeed, the force in a larger stone is greater. For coercive force, variation is due to greater resistance in larger subjects. With this premise, he argues: natural force tends to move infinitely, but since bodily force is divided with its subject, the whole force is stronger than a part. If the smaller part can move infinitely, then the whole either does not exceed it (contradiction) or exceeds it (increase upon infinite, impossible). If the smaller moves finitely, then by proportion the whole also moves finitely. Hence natural force is finite. Coercive force cannot be infinite because moving the whole from a point is less than moving a part from the same point, implying an infinite increase which is impossible. Finally, the author notes that this refutes an objection about celestial rotations being more numerous, affirming that such rotations are indeed subject to increase and decrease.

Key Terms
القوة الطبيعية
القوة الجسمانية
غير متناه

Checkpoint

A) It can produce infinite motion if the force is strong enough.
B) It cannot produce infinite motion because the ratio of forces leads to finite effect.
C) It is only effective in celestial spheres.
D) It is dependent on the receiver's resistance.

Key Takeaway
Natural bodily force cannot produce infinite motion because its effect is proportional to its subject's size, and any infinite scenario leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
8 min
Coercive Bodily Force Cannot Produce Infinite Motion
Demonstration that coercive force is finite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why coercive force cannot produce infinite motion due to increasing resistance in larger subjects.

Core Idea
Coercive force weakens with resistance, so effect on a larger body is less, not more, contradicting infinity.

Having established that natural force is finite, the author turns to coercive (forced) motion. He argues that coercive force's effect varies with the receiver's resistance: larger bodies have greater resistance, so the force's effect on the whole body from a given point is actually less than on a part. This makes infinite motion impossible. This argument is crucial for understanding the limits of forced motion in physics.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of coercive force
Coercive force is applied from outside; its effect on a body depends on the receiver's resistance. Larger bodies have more resistance, so the same mover produces less motion in a larger body.
This contrasts with natural force where the mover's force is proportional to the body's size.
2
Compare moving the whole body vs. a part from the same point
Consider a coercive force applied to a large body and to a small part of that body from the same starting point. The author claims that moving the whole body from that point is less than moving a part from the same point, because the whole has more resistance.
This is an empirical observation or assumed principle.
3
Assume for contradiction that coercive force could produce infinite motion
If the coercive force could produce infinite motion, then moving the whole body from a point would be an infinite motion. But moving the whole is less than moving a part (which would also be infinite if the whole is infinite, or at least finite).
The contradiction lies in the comparison: the whole's motion is supposed to be infinite yet less than a part's motion.
4
Derive the impossibility
Since moving the whole is less than moving a part, if moving the whole were infinite, then moving a part would be even more than infinite (impossible). Or if moving the part were finite, then moving the whole would be less than finite (also finite). So infinite motion is impossible.
The author states the conclusion directly.

Source Text

Refining Causation: Circularity from Opposites
Refutation of the objection from opposites to causal non-existence principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the circular reasoning (dawr) that arises when attributing an effect's non-existence to an opposite.

Core Idea
Attributing non-existence to an opposite leads to circularity unless traced back to the cause's non-existence.

The principle 'the non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of its cause' seems simple, but what about cases where an opposite appears? This passage addresses that objection and reveals a subtle circularity. Understanding this refines your causal reasoning in disciplines like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Principle and Objection
Identify the principle: 'The non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of its cause.' Then note the objection: sometimes the effect's non-existence seems due to an opposite, e.g., whiteness disappears because blackness appears.
2
Understand the Weak Response
Some say the opposite's non-existence was possible, so the effect's non-existence is still due to the cause's non-existence. The author rejects this because a non-existential cannot be part of the cause of existence.
This prepares the ground for the real problem.
3
Identify the Circularity
The real problem: The opposite's occurrence is conditioned by the removal of the effect's cause (they cannot coexist). If we explain the effect's non-existence by the opposite, we must explain the opposite by the effect's non-existence, leading to a vicious circle (dawr).
This is the key logical move.
4
Assess the Further Issue on Conditions
The author then asks whether every necessary condition for the effect is part of the cause. He says non-existence can be an obstacle but cannot be part of the efficient cause, otherwise composition would occur.
This links to usul al-fiqh discussions on 'illa and shart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: إن عدم المعلول قد يحصل لعدم العلة، لكنه قد يحصل أيضاً لحصول ضد كالبياض المنفى عن المحل لطروء السواد. والذي يقال من أن انتفاء ذلك الضد كان جائزاً، الأمر الذي لا يحصل إلا مع حصوله، فكان استناد عدمه عند طروء الضد تكون بالحقيقة انتفاء المعلول لانتفاء العلة، ضعيف؛ لأن العدمي لا يكون جزءاً من المؤثر في الوجود. بل الإشكال القوي فيه أن حدوث الضد الطاري مشروط بنوال الباغ، فلو عللناه بطرد الحادث لزم الدور. وهو محال. ثم في أنه هل كل ما لا بد منه في تحقق المعلول كان جزءاً من العلة؟ فيه إشكالان: بالعدم قد يكون معترضاً، ومع ذلك لا يجوز جعله جزءاً من المؤثر في الوجود، وإلا لزم وقوع التركب في العلة المؤثرة، وهو محال.
We say: The non-existence of the effect may occur due to the non-existence of the cause, but it may also occur due to the occurrence of an opposite, like whiteness being removed from a substrate due to the onset of blackness. And what is said—that the non-existence of that opposite was possible, and that it occurs only with its occurrence, so attributing its non-existence at the time of the opposite's occurrence to the non-existence of the cause is, in reality, weak—is weak because the non-existential cannot be part of the effect in existence. Rather, the strong problem in it is that the occurrence of the supervening opposite is conditioned by the removal of the seeker, so if we explain it by the removal of the occurrent, a vicious circle (dawr) follows, which is impossible. Furthermore, regarding whether everything necessary for the realization of the effect is part of the cause: There are two problems: non-existence may be an obstacle, yet it is not permissible to make it part of the effect in existence, otherwise composition would occur in the efficient cause, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the doctrine that the non-existence of an effect is always due to the non-existence of its cause (al-mashhur anna 'adam al-ma'lul li-'adam 'illatihi). The author acknowledges an objection: sometimes an effect's non-existence seems due to a positive cause such as an opposite (against) prevailing. For example, whiteness disappears because blackness arrives; the non-existence of whiteness is not due to the non-existence of its cause but due to the existence of an opposite. The author first dismisses a weak response: that since the opposite could have been non-existent, its actual existence is a condition for the effect's non-existence, so it reduces to the non-existence of the cause (since the opposite's non-existence would have allowed the effect to exist). This is weak because a non-existential (the opposite's non-existence) cannot be part of the cause that produces existence. Then he presents the real problem: the arrival of the opposite is itself conditioned by the removal of the effect's cause (since the opposite cannot coexist with the effect). Thus, if we try to attribute the effect's non-existence to the opposite's arrival, we need to explain the opposite's arrival by the removal of the effect's cause, leading to a circularity (dawr): the effect's non-existence is caused by the opposite's existence, and the opposite's existence is caused by the effect's non-existence. This circularity is impossible. Finally, the author raises a further issue: is every necessary condition for the effect's realization part of the efficient cause? He notes two problems: non-existence may be an obstacle, but it cannot be made part of the efficient cause, because that would imply composition in the efficient cause, which is impossible for simple causes. The discussion uses fiqh-style reasoning about causation and conditions, reminiscent of usul al-fiqh debates on 'illa and sharT.

Key Terms
عدم المعلول
عدم العلة
الضد
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it problematic to attribute the non-existence of an effect directly to the arrival of an opposite (ضد)?
A) Because the opposite is a non-existential and cannot be a cause of existence.
B) Because it leads to a vicious circle (dawr) since the opposite's arrival presupposes the effect's non-existence.
C) Because the opposite is itself caused by the effect's non-existence.
D) Because the cause of the opposite is unknown.

Key Takeaway
When analyzing non-existence, avoid circularity by ensuring that the cause of non-existence traces back to the absence of a positive cause, not to a dependent opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a kalam argument for the temporality of the world, suppose someone objects that the world's non-existence before creation was due to God's opposite (a counter-deity). Reconstruct this objection and show how it leads to circularity using the reasoning from this passage.
?
Task 2: In usul al-fiqh, consider a ruling that requires both a cause and a condition. Provide an example where confusing the absence of a condition with the absence of a cause leads to circular reasoning, and then correct it following the author's approach.

Refuting Inflow and Establishing Transmutation
Refutation of inflow and partial transformation; establishment of istihalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument by elimination that establishes istihalah by refuting the inflow and partial transformation explanations.

Core Idea
Transmutation is proven by eliminating alternatives: inflow and part-change.

The author defends the doctrine of substantial change (istihalah) by systematically refuting two alternative explanations: inflow of an external agent and partial transformation within a substance. Using concrete examples like sulfur ignition and water, he shows that only istihalah remains logically viable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternatives to istihalah
The author considers two alternative explanations for qualitative change: inflow (wurud) of an external agent into the body, and partial transformation of some parts of the body while others remain unchanged.
These are the main competing theories to istihalah.
2
Examine the refutation of inflow
The sulfur example: a small piece of sulfur near a flame quickly ignites entirely. If the ignition were due to inflow of heat from the flame, the sulfur would need to be larger than the flame to receive all that heat, which contradicts observation.
The argument shows that inflow cannot account for the observed change.
3
Examine the refutation of partial transformation
The water example: water parts are transparent and uniform. If heat transformed some parts into fire while leaving others cold, the transformation would be arbitrary. But observation shows that either all water is heated uniformly or not at all.
Thus, partial transformation is not a viable explanation.
4
Conclude istihalah by elimination
Since both alternative explanations are invalid, the only remaining explanation is substantial change (istihalah) – the body itself transforms into a new nature.
This is a logical deduction from the elimination of alternatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في النقود: وأما الورود منه فباطل من وجوه: أن جلا من كبريت المنار صغيرة قدر شعلة مصباح ثم بعد عنه بعجلة فيشعل كله، فإن كان بالورود عليه من الخارج، وجب أن يكون أكثر من تلك الشعلة. [؟] وإذا وضع [؟] فإذا كان ذلك [؟] ينفخ [؟] فيه من لك [؟] باطل، لأن الطبع ينفر عن الاتحاد ولا يتحرك إليه بالطبع، اللهم إلا أن يقال إن مجاورة ما سبب لحصول الاستعداد لذلك النفوذ، وذلك قول بالاستحالة.

ج[؟] إنه قد يتحن بالحركة وقد يضرب الإنسان فتحن بشرته من ورود فادية عليه، وإن انقلاب بعض أجزاء الماء [؟] باطل، لأن الأجزاء المائية شفافة فلا يكون بعضها بالانقلاب تاركاً أولى من الباقي، إلا أن العربي أولى بقبول الأنز[؟] من البعيد، فكان يجب إذا ظهرت الحرفة[؟] أن يظهر كل الحرفة[؟] بعض الماء من يصر بعضه كالنارق[؟] يبقى الباقي على مائه برودته لكن ليس إلا؟ وكذلك بل نظر بعض السخونة في كلا كنم[؟]. ولما بطلت الاحتمالات الثابت ثبت القول بالاستحالة.

ثم إن الحمد[؟] منع من إثبات الاستحالة لهذا النوع من الكيف مطلقاً بما ذكره، وأنه غير كاف، لأن ذلك إنما يدل على وقوع الاستحالة في خارة[؟] بعض الأجسام وبدود[؟] مما[؟]. فإن [؟] يقال: الناريح[؟] بقاء خارجيها تبرد خلاف الثلث[؟] الأرض مع بقاء أو ضيقه[؟] تصير رطبة لا على سبيل البلا[؟]، والماء يصير [؟] مع بقاء مائلته[؟] كينوسة[؟] الأرض لا عابير[؟] الشف، وأن الأسود يصير أبيض، وأن العلوم[؟] إلى غير ذلك من الكيفيات المحسوسة. فتلك [؟] لم يشتغلوا به أصلاً. نعم، مرتما[؟] حاولوا أن الأرض تتغلب على الماء أو بالعكس، ولكن ليس ذلك [؟] [؟].

فرق بين إثبات أن الأرض هل يمكن دراك[؟] الصورة الأرضية عند ويتصف بالصورة المائية، وبين أن يقال: الأرض مع بقاء صورتها الأرضية هل يمكن انصافها بالرطوبة. ثبت أن هذه المطالب لا بد من تصحيحها. ومرور آن ما ذكره[؟] المقام: أن تبدل هذه الكيفيات لا يوجد دفعة بل يسيراً. فما رأيت أحداً منهم اشتغل بالبرهان عليه، بل كانوا قنعوا فيه بتحسين[؟] من السفال[؟] المكر[؟] من البرودة إلى الحونة[؟]، واستقال الحرم[؟] من الحموضة إلى الحلاوة، ومن الخضرة إلى الحمة[؟] على سبيل التدريج. وأعلم أنه ليس كل ما حدث أكبر[؟] دفعة كان في الحقيقة كذلك، وبالعكس.

أما الأول: فلأن الشيخ [؟] حلى[؟] حجة[؟] أفاد الشعاع البصري الخارج، وهي أنه كان يجب أن تكون نسبة زمان حركة الشعاع إلى شرعى[؟] بعد ذراعين إلى زمان حركة إلى الثوابت نسبة المسافتين، فيجب أن يظهر بين الزمانين تفاوت عظيم، فكان يجب إذا فتحنا الجفن[؟] أن لا نرى الثوابت إلا بعد مدة. ثم إنه قال: من الممكن أن نفرض زماناً غير محسوس قصراً يحصل فيه حركة الشعاع إلى الثوابت، ثم إنه يمكن أن يتم ذلك الزمان إلى غير النهاية، ويكون جرم[؟] عظيم[؟] فيه [؟] ونسبته إليه نسبة المسافة القصيرة إلى المسافة البعيدة، فتلك النسبة تكون حاصلة مع أن الزمان العظيم لا يكون محسوساً. فقول[؟] بإن ليس كل ما كان في أكثر دفعة كان في الحقيقة دفعة. وأما العكس: فلات[؟] عنك الصورة الحيوانية تحدث دفعة مع أنا نرى المن[?] يتكون حواناً[?] بيع الترا[?]، وما ذاك إلا لأنه وإن كان مندرجاً في الحر[?] لكنه حاصل دفعة في الحقيقة. وإذ أثبت ذلك ظهر أنه لا يمكن التعويل على سرعة الحركات في هذه الكيفيات.
Discussion of objections: As for the inflow from it, it is false in several aspects: That a piece of sulfur from a lamp, small in size like the flame of a lamp, then separated from it quickly and ignites entirely. If it were by inflow from outside, it would be necessary that it be larger than that flame. And if it is placed [...] then if that is [...] blown into it from [...] it is false, because nature instinctively flees from union and does not move to it naturally, unless it is said that proximity to something is a cause for the acquisition of readiness for that penetration, and that is a statement of transmutation. [Another argument:] that it is affected by motion, and a person may be struck so his skin is affected by the arrival of heat upon it, and that the transformation of some parts of water [is false] because the watery parts are transparent, so some of them by transformation leaving [the form] is not more likely than the rest, except that the Arab [?] is more receptive to the more distant? So it would be necessary, if the heat appeared, that all the heat of some water would become like fire? while the rest remains in its water and coldness, but that is not the case. Similarly, [consider] the onset of heat in both? And when these possibilities are invalidated, the statement of transmutation is established. Then [the opponent] prevented the proof of transmutation for this type of quality absolutely by what he mentioned, and it is insufficient, because that only indicates the occurrence of transmutation in the transformation of some bodies and [??]. For it is said: 'The fire? remains its exterior becomes cold, unlike the third? The earth with its remaining [form] becomes wet not by way of transformation, and water becomes [??] with its inclination remaining as the earthiness? not [??], and that black becomes white, and that colors? to other sensible qualities.' So those [??] they did not occupy themselves with at all. Yes, they sometimes attempted that the earth overcomes water or vice versa, but that is not [??]. There is a difference between proving whether the earth can [??] the earthly form and be described by the water form, and between saying: the earth with its earthly form remaining, can it be characterized by wetness? It is established that these issues must be corrected. And [the point] that the change of these qualities does not occur instantaneously but gradually. So I have not seen anyone among them occupy himself with a proof for it, but rather they were content with a refinement? from the lowest? of cold to heat, and the transformation? from sourness to sweetness, and from greenness to black? in a gradual way. And know that not everything that occurs in more than an instant is in reality instantaneous, and vice versa. As for the first: because the Shaykh [Avicenna?] established an argument concerning the external visual ray, that it would be necessary that the ratio of the time of motion of the ray to a near distance of two cubits to the time of motion to the fixed stars be according to the ratio of the two distances, so there would appear between the two times a great difference. So it would be necessary, when we open the eyelid, that we do not see the fixed stars except after a period. Then he said: It is possible to assume an imperceptible short time in which the motion of the ray to the fixed stars occurs, then that time can be divided infinitely, and a large body [??] and its ratio to it is like the ratio of the short distance to the long distance, then that ratio is actual while the large time is imperceptible. So it is said that not everything that occurs in more than an instant is in reality instantaneous. As for the converse: consider the animal form which occurs instantaneously, while we see that the sperm becomes an animal gradually, and that is only because although it is subsumed under motion, it is actually instantaneous. And when that is proven, it becomes clear that one cannot rely on the speed of motions in these qualities.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a complex philosophical demonstration establishing the doctrine of istihalah (transmutation) and addressing an objection regarding the speed of qualitative change. The author begins by refuting the alternative explanation that change occurs by 'inflow' (wurud) of an external agent. He gives an example: if a small piece of sulfur ignites quickly when separated from a flame, its ignition cannot be due to inflow from the flame, because that would require a larger source. He also argues that nature does not naturally move towards union with another, except by a readiness produced by proximity—which itself implies transmutation. Next, he refutes the idea that change occurs by transformation of parts within a substance, using water as an example: since water parts are transparent, there is no reason why some parts would transform while others do not. Having invalidated these alternatives, he concludes that istihalah (substantial change) must be the correct explanation. After presenting an objection that his proof is insufficient—citing examples like fire becoming cold or earth becoming wet without changing substance—the author dismisses these as irrelevant to the nature of true istihalah. He then shifts to a key distinction: there is a difference between asking whether a substance can retain its form while acquiring new qualities, and asking whether change occurs gradually or instantaneously. He notes that many opponents rely on the observation that qualitative changes (e.g., from cold to hot) happen gradually. The author argues that gradual appearance does not disprove instantaneous reality. To support this, he invokes the argument of 'the Shaykh' (likely Avicenna) about visual rays: if light required time to travel, we would see a delay when looking at distant stars, but we do not, because the time is imperceptible. Similarly, the generation of an animal from sperm appears gradual but is actually instantaneous in terms of form acquisition. Thus, the speed of motion or gradual observation cannot be used to argue against istihalah.

Key Terms
استحالة
ورود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the ignition of a small piece of sulfur cannot be due to inflow from a larger flame?
A) Because sulfur cannot burn due to inflow; it requires direct contact.
B) Because if it were inflow, the sulfur would need to be larger than the flame to receive all the heat, which is not the case.
C) Because inflow only works for liquids, not for fire.
D) Because nature instinctively flees from union, so inflow is impossible.

Key Takeaway
When multiple explanations are possible, eliminating the false ones through careful argument can establish the correct one with logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar argument by elimination for the transformation of a liquid into vapor, using the alternatives of inflow, part-change, and substantial change. Explain why the first two are invalid, using the author's reasoning as a model.
?
Identify the logical structure of the argument: what are the premises and conclusion? Write them in standard propositional form.

Gradual vs Instantaneous Change: Perception vs Reality
Argument that gradual appearance does not disprove instantaneous reality; examples from vision and animal generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the apparent gradualness of change and its underlying instantaneous reality, using the author's arguments from visual rays and animal generation.

Core Idea
Gradual perception does not disprove instantaneous reality, as shown by light propagation and animal generation.

One objection to istihalah is that qualitative changes appear gradual, not instantaneous. The author counters by arguing that something can be instantaneous in reality yet appear gradual due to imperceptible speed or successive parts. He uses the examples of visual ray travel to distant stars and the generation of an animal from sperm to illustrate this principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Opponents argue that changes like cold to hot or sour to sweet occur gradually over time, so they cannot be instantaneous transmutations of substance.
The objection relies on observation: gradual appearance implies gradual reality.
2
Analyze the visual ray argument
The Shaykh (Avicenna) argues that if light traveled through a medium, the time to reach near objects vs far objects should be proportional. Yet we see stars instantly upon opening our eyes, implying either the speed is infinite or the time is imperceptible. He posits an imperceptibly short time.
This shows that an instantaneous-seeming event may actually have a finite but imperceptible duration.
3
Examine the animal generation example
The generation of an animal from sperm appears gradual (development over time), but the author argues that the animal form (surah hayawaniyya) is actually acquired instantaneously at the moment of conception, even though the material changes are gradual.
This directly parallels istihalah: the substance changes all at once, but the effects appear gradual.
4
Apply the principle to qualitative change
Similarly, when water heats up, the substantial change from cold to hot (istihalah) may occur instantaneously at the level of form, while the perception of gradual warming is due to the material substrate's successive reception of the new quality.
Thus, gradual observation does not refute istihalah.

Having invalidated these alternatives, he concludes that istihalah (substantial change) must be the correct explanation. After presenting an objection that his proof is insufficient—citing examples like fire becoming cold or earth becoming wet without changing substance—the author dismisses these as irrelevant to the nature of true istihalah. He notes that many opponents rely on the observation that qualitative changes (e.g., from cold to hot) happen gradually. The author argues that gradual appearance does not disprove instantaneous reality. To support this, he invokes the argument of 'the Shaykh' (likely Avicenna) about visual rays: if light required time to travel, we would see a delay when looking at distant stars, but we do not, because the time is imperceptible. Similarly, the generation of an animal from sperm appears gradual but is actually instantaneous in terms of form acquisition. Thus, the speed of motion or gradual observation cannot be used to argue against istihalah.

Classification of Forces and Refutation of Their Being First Cause
Classification and refutation of elemental forces as first causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's compound argument against elemental forces as first causes and identify the logical structure of the refutation (regress and dependency).

Core Idea
No elemental force can be a first cause: voluntary forces need a preponderator, natural forces require external interaction.

In this passage, the author classifies elemental forces into four types and then systematically argues that none can be the first cause of generation and corruption. The argument combines disjunctive reasoning, causal regress, and dependency on external conditions. This is a critical exercise in refuting a potential false cause in natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification of forces
The author divides elemental events: their principles are either elemental or not. If elemental, the forces are either inherent or not. Then a distinction is made between forces with awareness (voluntary) and without (natural). Natural forces are further divided into simple and acquired. Identify the four types mentioned: something like 'adversarial', 'neighborly', simple nature, and acquired nature.
The Arabic terms are uncertain due to OCR; focus on the logical categories.
2
Extract the refutation for voluntary forces
Voluntary forces can act or refrain; they need a preponderator to determine action. If that preponderator is also voluntary, infinite regress follows. If not, it ends in something else. Therefore, the voluntary force itself cannot be the first cause.
This is a classic regress argument.
3
Extract the refutation for natural forces (first reason)
Every element is subject to generation and corruption. If natural forces were first causes, the elements would be unchanging, but they are not. Heterogeneous bodies naturally repel each other, so interaction requires an external cause.
This argues from the nature of elements.
4
Extract the refutation for natural forces (second reason)
Experience shows natural forces only act when their conditions meet. Heterogeneous bodies do not meet by nature; they repel. Their meeting requires an external unifier, which cannot be elemental. Thus, natural forces depend on something else and are not first.
This is a causal dependency argument.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Since voluntary forces require a preponderator (leading to regress or dependence), and natural forces require external conditions and a unifier, none of the four types of elemental forces can be the first cause. The first cause must be transcendent.
The author does not name the first cause here, but the implication is clear for kalam.

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of forces and a refutation that any of them can be the first cause of generation and corruption in the sublunar world. The author first divides the principles of elemental events: if they are elemental, they must be either inherent in the bodies or not. If inherent, the forces operating them are either conscious (voluntary) or unconscious (natural). Natural forces are further divided into those that cause sinking (like fire and water) and others like magnetism. Thus four types emerge: something like 'adversarial'? (maybe 'adadiyya'), something like 'neighbor'? (qariyya), simple nature, and acquired nature. The author then argues against each being the first cause. Voluntary forces require a preponderator, leading to infinite regress or dependence on another. Natural forces in simple bodies are not first because elements themselves are subject to generation and corruption, so they cannot be ultimate; also, natural forces only act when their conditions meet, but heterogeneous bodies naturally repel each other, so their meeting requires an external unifier. That unifier cannot be elemental. Therefore, no elemental force is the first cause. The argument uses disjunctive reasoning (either voluntary or natural) and causal regress arguments.

Critique of the Proof for Time from Motion
Author critiques a proof for time from motion, exposing circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular reasoning (dawr) in attempted proofs and reconstruct the original argument to expose the circularity.

Core Idea
A proof that assumes the very thing it aims to prove is invalid, no matter how plausible it seems.

In this passage, the author critiques a common philosophical proof for the existence of time. The proof relies on differences in motion speed, but the author shows that the concepts of 'fast' and 'slow' already presuppose time. This is a textbook example of circular reasoning (al-dawr). Understanding how to detect such circularity is a key skill in logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The claim is that time exists, and the proof uses fast and slow motion. Determine what the author is objecting to.
2
Reconstruct the original argument
The unknown arguer says: (1) There is fast and slow motion; (2) they begin and end together; (3) they traverse the same distance. Therefore time exists. But note: each premise already involves time.
3
Pinpoint the hidden assumption
The definition of 'fast' is 'covers a distance in less time'. So using fast motion as a premise already uses the concept of time.
4
Apply the definition of circularity (dawr)
If a premise of the proof cannot be established without first establishing the conclusion, the argument commits dawr. Here, the premise 'there is fast motion' cannot be known without time.
5
Assess the validity
The argument is invalid because it assumes time to prove time. No valid demonstration can rely on such circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض على هذه الحجة: أما إثبات وجود الزمان فقد بنيتموه على أمور ثلاثة: أن هنا حركة سريعة وبطيئة، وأنهما يبتدئان معاً وينتهيان معاً، وأنهما يقطَعان مسافةً واحدةً إحداهما بعد الأخرى. وكل ذلك لا ينضبط إلا بعد ثبوت الزمان، لأن السريع هو الذي يقطع مثل ما قطعته البطيئة في زمان أقل، والبطيء بالعكس. وقولكم إنهما يبتدئان معاً وينتهيان معاً معناه أن ابتداء وجودهما وانتهاءه آن واحد، والآن لا يُعقل إلا بعد تعقل الزمان. فكذا القول بالقبلية والبعدية. فإذن دعوى وجود هذه الأمور لا تثبت إلا بعد ثبوت الزمان، فلو أثبتنا وجود الزمان بها لزم الدور.
The critique of this argument: As for proving the existence of time, you have based it on three things: that there is fast motion and slow motion, that they begin together and end together, and that they traverse one distance, one after the other. All of that cannot be determined except after affirming time, because the fast is that which traverses what the slow traverses in less time, and the slow is the opposite. Your statement that they begin together and end together means that the beginning of their existence and its end are one instant, and the instant is not conceptualized except after conceptualizing time. Likewise the statement regarding priority and posteriority. So the claim of the existence of these things is not established except after affirming time. If we were to prove the existence of time by means of them, circularity would follow.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a proof for the existence of time. The author identifies a logical flaw in the proof: it presupposes the very thing it aims to prove. The proof attempts to establish time by citing the phenomena of fast and slow motion, simultaneous beginnings and endings, and the traversal of equal distances at different speeds. But, the author argues, the definition of 'fast' and 'slow' already involves time—fast is that which covers a given distance in less time. Similarly, saying two motions begin 'together' implies an instant, which itself is understood only through time. Priority and posteriority likewise require time. Thus the proof is circular: it assumes time to prove time. This is a standard critique in Islamic logic, showing that certain proof attempts fail because they rely on the very concept under dispute.

السريع
البطيء
الآن
الدور

Key Takeaway
A valid proof must not rely on premises that presuppose the conclusion; circular reasoning invalidates the argument.

The Logical Structure of Geometric Proof by Contradiction
Author demonstrates a geometric proof using reductio ad absurdum and references Euclid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a proof by contradiction (burhan khalfi) in a geometric demonstration and explain how it leads to a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction: assume the opposite, derive an absurdity, and the original claim stands firm.

This passage from Mulakhkhas al-Razi presents a geometric proof that uses reductio ad absurdum to refute an objection. Despite the corrupted text, we can discern the logical method: the author assumes the opponent's claim and shows it leads to a contradiction, thereby proving the original assertion. This is a classic example of burhan khalfi, a staple of Islamic logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
What is the author trying to prove? In the passage, the claim is related to the possibility of constructing a circle or properties of lines and angles.
The exact claim is obscured by OCR, but the logical structure is clear.
2
Identify the opponent's assumption
The author assumes the opposite of the claim (e.g., that the line cannot be made into a circle or that certain geometric relations do not hold).
3
Trace the deduction of an absurdity
From the assumption, the author derives a consequence that is impossible (khulf), such as a side being equal to its chord or surfaces not meeting properly.
The text uses words like 'خلف' and 'باطل' to indicate absurdity.
4
Recognize the conclusion via contradiction
Since the absurdity is false, the assumption must be false, so the original claim is true. This is a conditional syllogism of the form 'if P then Q; not Q; therefore not P' (modus tollens).
5
Appreciate the use of geometric examples
The author uses Euclidean geometry to illustrate the method, showing that burhan khalfi is not limited to philosophy but applies to demonstrative sciences.

And a line of two sides and we place on one... another part connected there is a right angle, its chord if it is of two sides then the chord of the right angle is equal to each of the two surrounding sides, and if it is of three, then the chord is... and with the sum of the two sides this is a contradiction... this is much more... and less than... and Euclid proved... every line that is opened... its bisection, so the line composed of... by opening its bisection, it bisects the part, and he clarified... I explain the doubts of Euclid that every line that can be divided into three equal parts, so the line composed of two or four or... if we do similarly, then the triangles equilateral on a line of one hundred parts there emerges from one of its angles a line to the second part of the base which subtends it, obliging that triangle to divide into two triangles... proof of the following figure from the first book of Euclid, and the line emanating from that angle to that base is perpendicular, so each of these two triangles has a right angle, and the other angle in it is two-thirds of a right angle, and it is one of the angles of the first triangle, and the remaining is one-third of a right angle because it is half of one of the angles of the first triangle, so the chord of the right angle is... for three parts, and from the angle that is one-third of a right angle two parts, above the angle that is two-thirds of a right angle more than one part. If it is three, then the side of the right angle is equal to its chord, this is a contradiction. And if it is less than two-thirds, then the part has been divided. And in this triangle there are many other aspects that invalidate the chords. Euclid proved in the first book that parallelograms that are on the same base and in the same parallels are equal to each other, and that invalidates the parts, because if we suppose the boundary... four by four until its sum is sixteen, and the surface... from east to west, so it is possible that the sum of the many parts is equal to sixteen...
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a geometric demonstration (burhan) that employs proof by contradiction (qiyas khalfi) to refute an objection against a claim about circles and triangles. The author begins by considering a triangle and a certain condition, then leads to an absurdity if the circle cannot be constructed. He argues that if a line cannot be made into a circle, then a body with width cannot be a circle, and this leads to a chain of concentric circles reaching the size of the celestial sphere with no gaps. Then he considers the alternative that the outer surfaces do not meet while inners do, which leads to contradictions about angles and sides. The author then references Euclid's proofs about right angles and equilateral triangles, showing that assuming certain measurements leads to contradictions like a side being equal to its chord when it should not. He also mentions Euclid's proposition about parallelograms on the same base being equal, which undermines certain finite divisions. Throughout, the method is to assume the opponent's claim and derive an impossible consequence, thereby proving the original claim by contradiction. The examples are purely geometric, drawn from Euclidean geometry, which was a standard source for demonstrative reasoning in Islamic logic.

Proof by contradiction is a rigorous logical method that establishes a claim by showing the absurdity of its opposite.

Geometrical Proofs as Demonstrative Syllogisms
Euclidean geometrical proofs as demonstrative syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a demonstrative syllogism (burhan) in a geometrical proof from Euclid.

Core Idea
Geometrical proofs are demonstrative syllogisms: certain premises yield certain conclusions.

In this passage, al-Razi uses examples from Euclid's Elements to show how geometrical demonstrations exemplify the demonstrative syllogism (burhan). By analyzing these proofs, you will see how logical form underlies mathematical certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the geometrical proposition that the proof aims to establish, e.g., 'the hypotenuse of a right triangle with two equal sides is equal to each side'.
2
Find the premises
Locate the axioms, definitions, or previously proven theorems used as premises, such as definitions of right angle, hypotenuse, or Euclid's parallel postulate.
3
Determine the logical structure
Decide whether the argument uses categorical, conditional, or compound syllogisms. For example, a proof by contradiction often uses a conditional syllogism.
4
Check necessity
Verify that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises. If the premises are true and the reasoning is valid, the conclusion is certain.
5
Relate to burhan conditions
Confirm that the premises are yaqini (certain) and the syllogism yields yaqin, satisfying the definition of burhan in Islamic logic.

Commentary
This passage is a collection of geometrical proofs and discussions, likely drawn from Euclid's Elements and used by the author to illustrate demonstrative syllogisms (burhan) in logic. The author first considers a right triangle: if the right angle is formed by two equal sides, the hypotenuse equals each side (isosceles right triangle), but if the sides are unequal, the hypotenuse squared equals the sum of the squares of the sides (Pythagorean theorem). The text then discusses a triangle with angles of 90°, 60°, and 30°, and a perpendicular line from the right angle to the hypotenuse, creating two smaller triangles each with a right angle and angles of 60° and 30° respectively. The author shows that the length of the hypotenuse is divided into three parts, and the angle of 30° subtends twice the length of the angle of 60°, leading to a contradiction if the side is assumed equal to the hypotenuse. The author then references Euclid's proposition on parallelograms on the same base and between the same parallels being equal, and uses it to argue against certain divisions or constructions. The discussion continues with a problem about a square of area 16 and a rectangle of equal area but with variable length and width, noting that as length increases, width decreases, and that Euclid's proposition on equal areas does not apply when the base is not common. This serves as an example of how logical reasoning (burhan) is applied in geometry to prove or refute propositions.
