Refutation of Indivisible Parts in Motion: The Method of Khalaf
Author disproves Zeno's claim that motion composed of indivisible parts via contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction (khalaf) used to refute the claim that motion is composed of indivisible parts, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction shows that assuming indivisible parts in motion leads to logical absurdity, thus refuting the claim.

In this passage, the author takes on the ancient puzzle of Zeno: is motion composed of indivisible moments or parts? Using the method of khalaf (proof by contradiction), he demonstrates that such an assumption leads to a contradiction. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of his argument and the geometric reasoning that supports it. By mastering this technique, you will sharpen your ability to detect inconsistencies in physical and theological doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement the author is attacking: that motion is composed of indivisible parts (al-haraka tata'allaf min ajza' la tatajazza'). This is the target of the refutation.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction (khalaf)
Assume the claim is true. Then consider the distance traversed by an indivisible motion. If that distance is divisible, then the motion to half that distance is half the whole motion, making the original motion divisible — a contradiction. Therefore the distance must be indivisible, which itself may be problematic.
The author references an earlier discussion for the proof that the distance is divisible, but the key is the logical contradiction.
3
Analyze the geometric example
The author provides a concrete example: a sphere on a surface. The point of contact cannot be concave; it must be straight. Three reasons are given: (1) if concave, it would coincide with a concave surface, but the sphere is straight, contradiction; (2) Euclid's theorem: a line between two points on a circle lies inside; if concave, that line would be both inside and outside; (3) from the center, lines to contact points would create a triangle with impossible properties.
This geometric example reinforces the main refutation by showing another absurdity.
4
Extract the logical form
Identify this as a compound argument: first a simple khalaf (if distance divisible then motion divisible, contradiction), then a more elaborate geometric reductio. The overall conclusion is that the original claim (indivisible parts in motion) leads to absurdity, so it is false.
This is a classic use of proof by contradiction in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان ذلك محالاً كان القول بأن الحركة منتهية في القسمة إلى حيث لا تقبل القسمة محالاً أيضاً. وذلك لأن القدر الذي ينقطع بالحركة التي لا تقبل القسمة إن كان منقسماً كانت الحركة إلى نصفه نصف الحركة إلى كله، فتنقسم الحركة التي فرضناها غير منقسمة. لكن المقدم واجب الوجوه التي ذكرناها في باب الحركة تقرير الكلام زينون فلا نطول بالإعادة. فالتالى مثلاً لو وضعنا كرة على سطح موضع الملاقاة إما أن يكون مقعَّراً أو لا يكون. والأول باطل لثلاثة أوجه: أما أولاً فلأنه لو كان كذلك لانطبق على السطح المقعَّر، والمنطبق على المستقيم مستقيم، مع أن الكرة سطح مستقيم، هذا خلف. وأما ثانياً فلأن أوقليدس بيَّن أن كل خط مستقيم واصل بين نقطتين من الدائرة فإنه يقع داخلها، فلو كان موضع الملاقاة مقعَّراً لارتفع خط مستقيم على ظاهر الدائرة منطبق على السطح، فيكون ذلك الخط داخل الدائرة وخارجها. وأما ثالثاً فلأن موضع الملاقاة لو كان مقعَّراً أمكن أن تخرج من المركز خطان إلى نقطتي الملاقاة فيصيران مع الخط المرتسم موضع الملاقاة خطوطاً محيطة بكلّ، فيحصل هناك مثلث قاعدته...
Since that is absurd, the statement that motion terminates in division to where it does not accept division is also absurd. Because the amount that is traversed by motion that does not accept division – if it is divisible, then the motion to its half is half the motion to its whole, so the motion which we assumed to be indivisible becomes divided. But the premise is necessary in the ways we mentioned in the chapter on motion, containing the speech of Zeno, so we do not lengthen by repetition. For example, if we place a sphere on a surface, the point of contact is either concave or not. The first is false for three reasons: First, if it were so, it would coincide with the concave surface, and what coincides with a straight line is straight, while the sphere is a straight surface, this is a contradiction. Second, because Euclid showed that every straight line connecting two points of a circle falls inside it; if the point of contact were concave, then a straight line would rise on the exterior of the circle coinciding with the surface, so that line would be inside and outside the circle. Third, because if the point of contact were concave, it would be possible to draw from the center two lines to the two contact points, making with the line drawn at the contact point lines surrounding it, resulting in a triangle whose base...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that motion is composed of indivisible parts, often attributed to Zeno. The author begins by stating that if such a claim were true, it would lead to an absurdity. He then explains the reasoning: if the distance traversed by an indivisible motion is divisible, then the motion itself would be divisible, contradicting the assumption. The author refers to an earlier discussion for the proof of the premise. Then he provides a geometrical example involving a sphere and a surface to illustrate the absurdity. He gives three arguments against the possibility of the contact point being concave: first, a sphere is straight (not curved in the sense of being a straight surface, likely meaning a plane), so coincidence with a concave surface contradicts; second, following Euclid, a straight line between two points on a circle lies inside, so a concave contact would force a line both inside and outside; third, from the center, two lines to the contact points would create a triangle around it, leading to geometric impossibility. The passage is an example of proof by contradiction (خلف) in the context of natural philosophy.

حركة (haraka)
قسمة (qisma)

Key Takeaway

The Circularity of Indication as Proof of Actual Parts
Author refutes indication as proof of actual parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity in using indication (ishara) to prove that a simple body has actual parts.

Core Idea
If distinction depends on indication, indication cannot prove distinction.

In this passage, the author critiques an argument that tries to prove a simple body has actual parts by pointing to our ability to indicate its different sides. He reveals a hidden circularity: the argument assumes the very distinction it seeks to prove. Let's unpack this logical misstep.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that a simple body has actual parts because we can indicate different sides (e.g., left and right) of the body.
The proponent uses indication (ishara) as evidence for actual multiplicity.
2
Locate the author's objection
The author states that indication depends on the identity of the indicated thing. If the thing is not distinct in itself, indication is impossible. So the ability to indicate presupposes that the sides are already distinct.
The author says: 'الإشارة متوقف على المين في المشار اليه' – indication depends on the identity of the indicated.
3
Recognize the circularity
The argument tries to prove distinction by indication, but indication already requires distinction. This is circular: the premise assumes the conclusion.
The author explicitly warns: 'لو جعلنا التميز في المشار اليه معللا بالاحتيان في الاشارة لزم الدور' – if we make distinction in the indicated dependent on the need for indication, circularity results.
4
Apply to a kalam example
Consider the Ash'arite atomist view: they argued that atoms are indivisible because no further indication is possible. The author's critique shows that this reasoning would be circular if atoms were said to have no parts because they cannot be indicated.
This helps distinguish valid from circular use of indication in metaphysical proofs.

لا يضاد المقادير والجمات كان عرفت لكن هناك هيولى شركة لانا تقوك تلك الهيولى ان كانت عند وحدة الجزر واحدة فعند القيمة قد انضمت فيعود المحاك وان كانت مستعدة لحبيب الانفتاحات المكنه فالمقدار والحمية الحالة في كل واحد منها عنى الحالية الآف فتكون الأور اسمين الكل واحد منها عن الآخرى بمادته وصورته ويعود الحال، اذ انظرتُ إلى النجم البسيط أعلم بالضرورة ان احد تصفيه مغاير للنصف الآخر وممتاز عنه فكيف يمكن مع ذلك الحكم بكرن ذلك الجسم و احد ان الحقيقة ولايقاك لم لايجون ان يقال الكثرة بالفعل انا حصلت بسبب التوم او الخلاف الأعراض انا اقوال التمر في الإشارة متوقف على المين في المشار اليه الاستحالة ان يمتنع الإشارة ماليس التثمين في ذاته فلو جعلنا التميز في المشار اليه معللا بالاحتيان في الاشارة لزم الدور وكذا الفوك فيما يقولونه من وقوع الاستيان بسبب اختلاف العراض وايضا فانا نعا بالضرورة ان احد جانح هذا الحجم قد كان متان عن جانبنه الآو سوار كان يتوهم متوهم یا اولم يتوهم فالقول بات هذا الامتياز الما حصل حال التوهم معايرة و اذا حلمت عنا البحر البسيط جانه موجود ف المحكوم عليه ليس الا المجلة المتخلة من الأبعاض المفروضة فيه فلو قلنا ان تلك الابعاض أمور تحدث عند القمة وان الموجود قبل القسمة المرور آن تلك الابعاض كان ذلك خروجا عن المعقول لانا لا نعقل من جملة هذ النجم الى المجموعة الحاصل من اجوان و جوانيه مثبت بهذه الوجوه ان اجوا النجم حاصلة بالفعل لع انا حالة الاتصال غير ملتفاصلة بالفعل قلت اجد ابا الفعلاني ليس انفصال بعض عن البعض حاصلا بالفعل ومعلوم ات الدلالة التي ذكرناها لاتختلف حالها بان كانت الأجر استفاصلة او متواصلة الثاني حلمت ان الجمر في نفسمت واحد بالفعل لكن الحجة المذكورة فاذا كان قول القسمة على هذا التقديرم ا غير عند فعة الضامن وحمين احدها الجسم . تحرك ماجنات طرق المتحرك ابدا بلغ من المحرك عليه شيبا غير القيه قبل ذلك والخلاف الاعراض المضافة عندكم سبب لحدوث القسمة فالقائم ابدا قائم و الحدوث القمة فالقاسم ابدا قائم والمنقما بدا قابل موجب ان يحدث فيه القسامات بالفعل غير متناهية لانو حتى صار طلاقت الحد من حدود المسافة فقد حدث ذلك احد وقبل ذلك الحد كان ملاقي الحد آخر بالفعل وقبل ذلك احد لحد آخر غير مناه فلا نفض حدا الا وقد حدث قبله حدود الغير مشاهية بالفعل بسبب الملاقيات المتعاقبة وجنيد يعود المحالات اختلاف الأعراض الإضافية عندكم موجب لحدوث الانقسام بالفعل وكلجم موجود فانه لابد وان يلاقى بالخلط فيه شيبا غير ما لاقيه الطرف الآف فيلم ان حدث ذلك الحجم القسام بالفعل ثم ان احد نصفيه بلاقي احد وجميه شاعر طلاقته بوجه الآخر فيلام ان ينتصف ذلك النصف ايضا فاة لا كان قبول المنقم النفير حاصلاً ابدا وكان المقيم بالفعل هو الخلاف الماسات حاصلا بالمعمل ابدا لزم محصول للانقسامات بالفعل وظهرات الجم لوكات قابلا انفتامات غير متناهية كانت تلك الانفامات بأسرها حا صلة بالفعل انقال هذا اللوجة الخير سبي على ان الجسم إذ التي باحد طرفيه شيا غير الذي لقيه بطرفه الآخر فانه ينتصف ذلك لنجم وذلك باطل بل عيتان احد جانبية عن الآن بالفعل وجابتة سطحة وهو عامر لانا نقول لا شكر انه اذالقی با حد جانبية غير القيه بجانبنه الآخر فقد امتان الحجابنيه عن الآن بالفعل فالجانبان اما ان تكون المرجع بهم الى العرضين القائمين به او الى جزء ی خانه فان كان الاول وجد اختيار على ذيك العرضين فان كان ذلك المحل حضا لزم التسلسل وان انتمى ذلك الى جزءى ذاته وهو القسم الثاني فقد توجة الإشكال فهذا اتمام هذه الحجه وَهُرُ آخر الكنفي
Quantities and bodies do not contradict [each other]... but there is a shared matter. For we say: that matter, if it is of one essence when [something] is one, then at division it joins and the problem returns. And if it is disposed to various divisions, then the quantity and the quality in each one of them... So the whole [body] would be composed of individuals each different from the other in matter and form, and the problem returns. When I consider the simple body, I know necessarily that one half is different from the other half and distinct from it. So how can it be judged that that body is one in reality? And you say: why is it not said that multiplicity actually arises due to division or difference of accidents? We say: indication depends on the identity of the indicated thing; it is impossible to indicate what is not [something] in itself. If we make distinction in the indicated thing dependent on the need for indication, a circularity results. Similarly, what they say about continuity occurring due to difference of accidents. Also, we know necessarily that one side of this volume was distinct from its other side, whether someone imagines it or not. So the claim that this distinction only occurs in the state of imagination is false. And if we imagine the simple sea as actually existing, what is ruled upon is nothing but the whole composed of the assumed parts. If we say that those parts come into being at division and that what existed before division was not those parts, that is contrary to reason, for we do not understand from the whole of this body except the aggregate resulting from its sides. With these arguments it is proven that the sides of the body are actual, but we say that the state of connection does not separate them actually. I say: the actuality is not separation of some from others actually, and it is known that the indication we mentioned does not differ whether the parts are separate or connected. Secondly, imagine that the body is divided actually into one, but the aforementioned argument... So if the statement of division is upon this estimation, it is different when considering the parts? One of them is the body. A moving thing... the mover always reaches something from the moved thing that it did not reach before. According to you, difference of relational accidents is the cause of division. So the divider is always dividing, and the divided is always divisible, necessarily giving rise to actual infinite divisions. Because when it meets a limit of the limits of distance, that limit is actual, and before that limit it was meeting another actual limit, and before that another limit ad infinitum. So no limit occurs except that infinite other limits have already occurred actually due to successive meetings. And then the impossibility returns. According to you, difference of relational accidents causes actual division. Every existing body necessarily meets with a part something other than what its other side meets. Then it follows that the body is actually divided. Then each of its halves meets one of its sides... similarly that half must also be divided. Since the divisible is always divisible and the actual division is always occurring due to actual difference, actual divisions must result, and the bodies are capable of infinite divisions, so all those divisions are actual. This last argument is based on the idea that when the body meets with one end something that it does not meet with the other end, then that body is divided. That is false. Rather, one of its sides is actual and its other side is actual and... We say: there is no doubt that when it meets with one side something other than what it meets with the other side, then the two sides are distinct actually. The two sides either refer to the two accidents subsisting in it or to a part of it. If the first, then distinction occurs in those two accidents. If that locus is a part, an infinite regress follows. If it refers to a part of its essence, which is the second case, then the problem arises. This completes this proof, and it is the end of the discussion.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a critical philosophical argument refuting the claim that a simple body has actual parts. The author addresses arguments based on division and indication. The discussion begins by considering the nature of a simple body: if we assume a shared matter, problems arise when division occurs. The author then questions how a simple body can be one in reality if its halves are distinct. He refutes the idea that multiplicity arises from division or accidental differences, pointing out that indication of a part would lead to circularity if distinction depends on indication. He argues that even without imagination, sides of a body are distinct, so distinction is not merely imaginary. He further argues that if we consider the body as a whole composed of assumed parts, and then claim parts only come into being at division, that is irrational. The author then considers the argument that actual division follows from difference of relational accidents: if a body meets something at one end not met at the other, it must be divided. But the author rejects this, saying that distinction of sides does not entail actual division into parts; the sides may refer to accidents or to parts, leading to infinite regress. The overall proof concludes that actual infinite division is impossible and the initial arguments are unsound. The author uses the technical vocabulary of 'simple body', 'actual division', 'indication', 'relational accidents', and 'infinite regress'.

Key Terms
الجسم البسيط
القسمة بالفعل
الإشارة
الأعراض الإضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw the author identifies in the argument that indication proves actual parts?
A) It assumes just causation without evidence.
B) It commits circular reasoning (دور).
C) It relies on a false analogy.
D) It ignores the possibility of imagination.

Key Takeaway
Indication cannot be used to prove actual parts because it already presupposes the distinction it seeks to establish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Evaluate the argument: 'I can point to the left and right sides of this stone, therefore the stone has two distinct parts.' Use the author's reasoning to identify the circularity.
?
2. In kalam, the mutakallimun argued that atoms are indivisible because no further indication is possible. Analyze whether this use of indication avoids the circularity or commits the same error.

Infinite Regress from Relational Accidents
Author refutes argument from relational accidents leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the infinite regress argument that results from claiming relational accidents cause actual division.

Core Idea
If relational accidents cause actual division, then division never stops and actual parts are infinite.

Another argument for actual parts claims that when a body meets different things at its ends, it must be actually divided. The author shows that this leads to an infinite regress of divisions, which is impossible. Let's trace the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial claim
The claim is that difference of relational accidents (e.g., meeting different objects at opposite ends) causes actual division of the body.
This is attributed to opponents (عندكم).
2
Follow the first step of the regress
If a body meets one object at its left side and a different object at its right side, then the left side and right side are distinct. According to the claim, this distinction implies actual division into two parts.
The author says: 'اذا لقی باحد جانبيه غير الذي لقيه بجانبه الآخر فقد امتاز الجانبان عن الآن بالفعل' – when it meets with one side something other than what it meets with the other side, the two sides become actually distinct.
3
Identify the repeated application
Now each of those two parts (the left half and right half) also has sides. Each half will meet different things at its own ends, so each half must be divided again. This process continues without end.
The author says: 'ثم ان احد نصفيه بلاقي احد وجميه ... فيلام ان ينتصف ذلك النصف ايضا' – then each half must be divided again.
4
Reach the conclusion of infinite actual parts
Since the body is always divisible and the condition for division (difference of accidents) is always present, the body would have infinitely many actual divisions simultaneously. This is impossible, so the original claim is false.
The author concludes: 'لزم محصول للانقسامات بالفعل وظهرات الجم لوكات قابلا انفتامات غير متناهية كانت تلك الانفامات بأسرها حا صلة بالفعل' – actual divisions result and bodies capable of infinite divisions would have all those divisions actual.

Source Text

Quantities and bodies do not contradict [each other]... but there is a shared matter. For we say: that matter, if it is of one essence when [something] is one, then at division it joins and the problem returns. And if it is disposed to various divisions, then the quantity and the quality in each one of them... Commentary
This passage is a critical philosophical argument refuting the claim that a simple body has actual parts. The author addresses arguments based on division and indication. The discussion begins by considering the nature of a simple body: if we assume a shared matter, problems arise when division occurs. The author then questions how a simple body can be one in reality if its halves are distinct. Checkpoint
What is the impossibility the author derives from the claim that relational accidents cause actual division?
A) The body would have a finite number of parts.
B) The body would have infinitely many actual parts simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The claim that relational accidents cause actual division leads to an absurd infinite regress, so it cannot be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Apply the author's regress argument to a moving body: A car moves from point A to point B. As it moves, it meets different parts of the road. According to the relational accident claim, the car should be actually divided at each moment. Show the infinite regress and why it is impossible.
?
2. In kalam, some argued that atoms are indivisible because they are not subject to relational accidents in a way that causes division. Assess whether this avoids the regress or introduces a new problem.

The First Proof for Prime Matter Using Conditional Reasoning About a Point
Author presents first proof for prime matter using conditional reasoning about a point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional (reductio) argument for prime matter's existence by analyzing the disjunction about points and the temporal instantiation of corporeality, and identify where each branch leads to an absurdity.

Core Idea
Prime matter is proven by reducing its denial to absurd consequences about points and temporality.

This passage presents one of four proofs for hylomorphism, the doctrine that all bodies are composed of prime matter and form. The argument uses a conditional (reductio ad absurdum) structure: assuming prime matter exists devoid of form, it must be either something that can be pointed to (i.e., it has spatial location) or not. We will follow the logical steps as al-Razi examines each branch, showing that each leads to an impossibility, thereby establishing the necessity of prime matter as the underlying substratum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main disjunction
The argument begins with a disjunction: if matter is devoid of form, it is either 'pointed to' (has location) or not. This splits the inquiry into two mutually exclusive branches.
This is reminiscent of the logical operation of dividing a concept into its contraries, a common technique in Islamic dialectic (jadal).
2
Examine the 'pointed to' branch: divisibility
If matter can be pointed to, it must be either divisible or indivisible. If indivisible, it would be a point (nuqta). The argument then shows that an independent point is impossible through a geometrical reductio involving two lines meeting at the point.
The point is used as a counterfactual: it is not meant to assert that matter actually is a point, but to show that if it were, contradictions arise.
3
Analyze the point reductio
If two lines come to the point, either the point lies between their endpoints (making it divisible) or the endpoints are inside it (making them separate from the point). Both lead to contradiction with the definition of an indivisible point.
This step critically depends on understanding the geometry of points and lines, a common tool in falsafa and kalam.
4
Consider the alternative: matter is divisible/extended
If matter is divisible (not a point), it must be a body. But then corporeality (jismiyya) must inhere in it either at a specific time or at all times. Each sub-branch is examined and found to be impossible.
Here the argument shifts from geometry to temporality, using principles of causation and necessity.
5
Conclude the absurdity
Both branches lead to absurdities, so the original assumption (that matter exists devoid of form) is false. Therefore, matter does not exist as a separate entity; rather, it exists as the substratum that receives forms, proving hylomorphism.
This is a classic reductio: deny the conclusion and show that it leads to contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن النساء خلق الهيولى عن العودة واحتجوا عليها بوجوه أربعة: فال كانت الهيولى خالية لكانت إما أن تكون مشاراً إليها أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول: فإما أن تكون قابلة للقسمة أو لا تكون. فإن لم تقبل القسمة كانت نقطة. لكن وجود النقطة على الاستقلال محال، لأنه إذا أتى إليها خطان فيها فهي إما أن تجيب بين القطتين اللتين هما طرفا الخطين فتصير منقسمة على ما مر في مسألة الجزء، أو لا تجيب فالتقطتان داخلتان فيها وهي بائنة عن الخطين، فالتقطتان اللتان هما طرف الخطين مبائنتان عنها، وهذا خلاف. وكذا للفوك في إبطال كونها خطاً أوسط. وفي حال كونا جماً لمامرات النجم لابد له من الهيولى. وإن تكن مشاراً إليها فحينئذ حلت الجمية فيها: فإما أن تحصل في حين معين، وهو محال، لأن المقصود لذلك الحين إما الجمية العامة أو لوازمها، وهو محال لأنه إذا حصل كلجم فيه أو أمر غير لازم، فحينئذ لا يجب عند حصول الجمية حصول ذلك الأمر، فلنفرض عدم حصوله، وحينئذ يسجل [؟] إن مختصر حير معين دون الحيات، فإما أن تحصل في جميع التجار أو في بعض الأحيان مع أننا قد تحمت، وذلك أيضاً محال. فقد ثبت أن القول بخلق الهيولى عن للجمية يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة.
For the proponents of matter from [؟] adduced four proofs: If matter were devoid [of form], it would either be pointed to or not. If the first: then either it is divisible or not. If it is not divisible, it is a point. But the existence of a point independently is impossible, because if two lines come to it, it is either between the two points which are the ends of the lines, thus becoming divided as previously in the issue of the part, or it is not between them, then the two points are inside it and it is separate from the lines, so the two points which are the ends of the lines are separate from it, and this is contrary. Likewise, for the [؟] in refuting its being a middle line. And in the case of [؟] it becomes a mass, then it must have matter. And if it is pointed to, then corporeality occurs in it: either it occurs at a specific time, which is impossible, because the purpose for that time is either universal corporeality or its concomitants, and that is impossible, because if every [؟] occurs in it or a non-necessary thing, then it is not necessary upon the occurrence of corporeality that that thing occurs, so let us suppose its non-occurrence, and then it is recorded [؟] that [؟] at a specific time without the [؟], so it either occurs at all times or at some times, while we have [؟], and that is also impossible. So it is established that the claim of creating matter from corporeality leads to these false divisions.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of prime matter (al-hayula) in the context of hylomorphism. The argument is structured as a conditional reasoning: assume that matter exists devoid of any form. Then it must either be something that can be pointed to (i.e., it has spatiality) or not. If it can be pointed to, then it is either divisible or indivisible. If indivisible, it would be a point. But an independent point is impossible because if two lines intersect at it, it would either lie between their endpoints, making it divisible (contradicting indivisibility), or the endpoints would be inside it, making them separate from the point—impossible. Next, if it is divisible but held to be a line, that too is refuted. If it is not point-like but extended, then it must be a body (jism). But then corporeality (jismiyya) would enter it at some specific time or at all times. If at a specific time, a cause for that time is needed: either corporeality itself or something else; if corporeality itself, then it would exist always, contradicting the assumption; if something else, then it could fail to occur, making the time arbitrary. If at all times, then matter would always have form, contradicting the idea of prime matter as pure potentiality. Thus, the claim that matter is created without form leads to absurdities, proving the necessity of prime matter's existence.

Key Terms
هيولى (hayula): Prime matter, the underlying substratum that receives forms, itself devoid of all forms. [Note: In earlier Greek tradition, hyle; in Islamic philosophy, it is the first matter, often contrasted with 'sura' (form).]
نقطة (nuqta): An indivisible point, used here as a reductio: prime matter cannot be a point because points cannot exist independently. [Note: In geometry, point is a limit; here used argumentatively to deny independent existence.]
جسمية (jismiyya): Corporeality, the form of body (jism) that makes matter extended. [Note: Also used in kalam as a category; here it is the form that inheres in matter.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is either a point or divisible.
B) It is either pointed to or not.
C) It is either body or spirit.
D) It is either created or eternal.

Key Takeaway
The existence of prime matter is proven by showing that its denial leads to absurdities regarding points and temporal instantiation of form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct al-Razi's argument by writing out each conditional step and identifying the absurd consequence it leads to. Then check whether the argument is valid by the rules of conditional reasoning.
?
Consider a parallel dialectical argument: 'If the Qur'an is created, then it is either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then it is co-eternal with God (impossible). If it is originated, then there must be a moment of its origination, leading to a temporal regress (impossible). Therefore, the Qur'an is uncreated.' Identify the conditional steps and their validity.

Critique of the Proof for Substantial Forms
Al-Razi attacks the proof for substantial forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's argument that matter suffices for explaining accidents without substantial forms, and identify the circularity he exposes.

Core Idea
Al-Razi dismantles the proof for substantial forms by showing matter alone can account for accidents.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi challenges the very foundation of Aristotelian physics: the doctrine of substantial forms. He argues that the logicians' proof for these forms fails because matter itself can explain the connection between shape and magnitude, and that positing forms leads to logical absurdities like circularity and violation of the one-cause-one-effect principle. This lesson unpacks his systematic deconstruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the claim that substantial forms are necessary to explain the concomitance of accidents in bodies. The author states: 'if matter is sufficient for the concomitance of form, why is it not sufficient for accidents?'
2
Follow the first objection: matter suffices
Al-Razi argues that matter itself can account for the connection between shape and magnitude, without needing a form. He uses the example of elements that accept shapes easily (moist) or with difficulty (dry), which can be explained by existent or absent causes.
3
Understand the circularity argument
Forms are either in the body or in matter, but both require the body to exist. If the body were caused by forms, circularity arises (body → form → body). This is a logical refutation.
4
Analyze the dilemma of one vs. many forms
If one form produces multiple effects (quality, quantity, shape), it violates the principle that one cause yields one effect. If many forms are posited for one matter, that is also rejected. The attempt to order forms fails because the accidents are not ordered.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that the proof for substantial forms is insufficient; no one has demonstrated that these forms are causes of the body's existence.

Source Text

والمقدار. فتعين أن كون ذلك اللزوم من المادة. وإذا كانت المادة كافية في لزوم ملك الصورة فلم لا يكفي في لزوم هذه الأعراض من غير حاجة إلى إثبات هذه الصور؟ وأما العناصر مع أن منها ما يقبل الأشكال بسهولة فهو الرطب ومنها ما يقبلها بصعوبة وهو اليابس. إذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال إن سهولة قبول المشكلات معللة بعلة وجودية، وأما صعوبة قبولها فإنها معللة بعلة عدمية وهي عدم تلك العلة أو بالعكس؟ وعلى هذا لكن بشرط حلول الجثة فيها، وعلى التقديرين فاتقان وجودها محتاجة إلى الحمية. فلو كانت الجثة معللة بها لزم الدور. ثم إما أن يقول في الجسم صورة واحدة هي عين ما فيه من الكيف والمقدار والشكل، فقد حكموا بأنه قد صدر عن الصورة الواحدة أكثر من أثر واحد وهو عندهم منكر. أو يثبتوا [؟] الجسم حسب ما كان عرض مخصوص صورة مخصوصة، فقد حكموا بإثبات الصور الكثيرة للمادة الواحدة وذلك عندهم ممتنع. لا يقال: هذه الصور مرتبة. لأنا نقول: هذه الأعراض مترتبة فانه ليس بأن [؟] يقال إنصاف النار باليبوسة أولى من العكس فإنا نجد كل واحد منها منفكا عن الأخرى. وإذا [؟] لم يكن بين هذه الآثار ترتب وجب أن لا يكون بين عليها ترتب أيضا فلا تكون تلك الصور مرتبة. ويعود الإشكال. الثانية: الأحكام العامة [؟] وهي أنها [؟] لكل جسم حتى [؟] طبعا. هذا هو المشفر عند المتأخرين. أما أولا فلو قدرناه خاليا عن جميع العوارض المفارقة فإما أن يحصل كل الأمكنة أو لا، وكلاهما محالات. وأما كان معينا فيكون ذلك المكان هو المكان الطبيعي. وقانون [؟] العاشر في الجسم كأنه معينا لكان استدعاؤه له إما أن يكون لجملته أو لوازمه فيكون كل جسم فيه. وإن كان لأمر غير لازم في الجسم فانصاف ذلك الجسم بذلك الأمر إن كان الأمر محالا وبتقدير جوازه فلنجوز أيضا أن يكون الكون له من الأمانة مستحقا له. ثم إنه يحصل على سبيل الاتفاق واحد عنها أو لا. فإن كان مقارنا كان يكون أن يقال إنما عاد لكونه طالبا
The vegetative soul and not any of its concomitants, nor that which is not a state in it nor a locus for it, because all that is false. Even if it were granted, the purpose is achieved, because if the concomitance of those forms were due to these divisions, they would be like the concomitance of shape and quantity. So it is determined that this concomitance is from matter. And if matter is sufficient for the concomitance of the form, why is it not sufficient for the concomitance of these accidents without need to prove these forms? As for the elements, some of them accept shapes easily—that is moist—and some accept with difficulty—that is dry. Since this is the case, why is it not said that easy acceptance of shapes is caused by an existent cause, and difficult acceptance by a non-existent cause, which is the absence of that cause, or conversely? On this estimation, the impossibility of creating bodies is not apparent from the substantial form. Moreover, it is necessary [??] to attribute them to an existent knowledge. So why is it said that that [??] form is an expression of the constitutive state of the subject up to the state that is a cause for the existence of its subject? And if you understand this, then the mentioned proof indicates the causation of these accidents. And there are existent things in the bodies, but there must be an indication that those things are causes for the existence of those bodies, so that those things are considered forms. Otherwise, they are never forms. And I have not yet seen any of them attempt to prove this claim. But granted that the mentioned proof yields the proof of substantial forms, yet here there is something that indicates their negation from two aspects: these forms are either inherent in the body or in the hyle but on condition that the body is inherent in them; on both estimations, their existence is in need of the body. So if the body were caused by them, there would be circularity. Then either they say in the body there is one form that is identical to what is in it of quality, quantity, and shape—they have ruled that more than one effect originates from one form, which is for them denied. Or they establish the body according to a specific accident and a specific form—they have ruled to prove many forms for one matter, and that is for them impossible. It cannot be said: these forms are ordered. For we say: these accidents are ordered, for it is not that [??] the attribution of fire to dryness is more appropriate than the opposite, for we find each of them separate from the other. And if there is no order among these effects, it is necessary that there be no order among them as well, so those forms are not ordered. And the problem returns. Second: the universal judgments [??] that they are for every body until [??] naturally. This is the famous [view] among the later [scholars]. As for first, if we suppose it devoid of all separable accidents, then either it obtains every place or not, both are impossible. And if it is determinate, then that place is the natural place. And the law of the tenth [??] in the body, as if it were determinate, its demand for it would be either due to its totality or its concomitants, so every body would be in it. And if it is due to something non-necessary in the body, then the attribution of that body to that thing, if that thing is impossible, and if its permissibility is granted, let it also be permissible that the existence of it from the trust is worthy of it. Then it occurs by way of coincidence, either one of them or not. If it is concomitant, then the discussion about it is like the first. Or it is prior to it in the sense that the prior [??] the subsequent for being described with the obtained attribute. And if that is permissible, let it be said also that the body's existence in a determinate place was only because, before obtaining that place, it was in another place or described with another attribute for which it obtains this place. Even if natural entitlement is not present. Do you not see that if we push a stone upward, it returns? And were it not that it is by nature a seeker of the center, otherwise it would not be so. And the objector says: why can it not be said that it returns because it is a seeker?
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the doctrine of substantial forms (al-ṣuwar al-nawʿiyya), a key concept in Aristotelian natural philosophy. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) attacks the logicians' proof for the necessity of these forms. He begins by dismissing the idea that forms are needed to explain the concomitance of accidents. He argues that matter itself can account for the connection between shape and magnitude, so why posit additional forms? He then uses the example of elements: moist things accept shapes easily, dry things with difficulty. This difference can be explained by a cause (either existent or absent) without invoking a form. He challenges the notion that the proof for forms actually shows they are causes of the body's existence, noting that no one has demonstrated this. Even if the proof were accepted, he offers a dilemma: either the form is in the body or in matter, but since both need the body, circularity results (body would cause form and vice versa). Moreover, if one form produces multiple effects (like quality, quantity, shape), that violates the principle that one cause yields one effect. If multiple forms are posited for one matter, that is also rejected. The author then refutes the possible defense that forms are ordered, by showing the corresponding accidents are not ordered. Finally, he turns to the concept of natural place (al-makān al-ṭabīʿī), critiquing the later philosophers' idea that bodies have a natural tendency. He argues that if a body is stripped of all accidents, its location is either indeterminate or determinate, both problematic. The movement of a stone upward and returning can be explained by its being a seeker of the center, but this is the very point under debate: whether natural place is intrinsic or acquired. The commentary shows the rigorous dialectical method: the author anticipates objections and systematically undermines the foundational assumptions of the opposing view.

المادة
العرض
الهيولى
الجثة

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's critique shows that the proof for substantial forms is logically insufficient, relying on assumptions that are neither proven nor necessary.

?

Critique of the Doctrine of Natural Place
Al-Razi critiques the concept of natural place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Razi's objections to the concept of natural place and evaluate his argument that bodies' motion can be explained without intrinsic tendencies.

Core Idea
Al-Razi undermines the natural place doctrine by showing it leads to contradictions and is not demonstrable.

Al-Razi continues his assault on Aristotelian physics by turning to the concept of natural place (al-makān al-ṭabīʿī). He questions how a body stripped of accidents could have a determinate place, and whether the tendency to move is intrinsic or acquired. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications for natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine under critique
The doctrine holds that every body has a natural place determined by its essence, and it moves toward that place by nature. Al-Razi quotes the later philosophers' view.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Suppose a body stripped of all separable accidents. Could it be in all places or no place? Both are impossible, so the body must be in a determinate place. But what determines that place?
3
Analyze the basis for natural entitlement
If the body's determinate place is due to its totality or concomitants, then every body would be in that place, which is false. If due to a non-necessary attribute, that attribute would need to be specified, leading to infinite regress or coincidence.
4
Examine the stone example
Al-Razi uses the example of a stone pushed upward that returns downward. The standard explanation is that it seeks its natural place (the center). But he questions: why can't this be due to a prior state (e.g., being displaced) rather than an intrinsic tendency?
5
Conclude the critique
The objector's response (the stone returns because it is a seeker) is circular. The doctrine of natural place is not proven and leads to contradictions when stripped of accidents.

Source Text

لكن بشرط حلول الجثة فيها، وعلى التقديرين فاتقان وجودها محتاجة إلى الحمية. فلو كانت الجثة معللة بها لزم الدور. ثم إما أن يقول في الجسم صورة واحدة هي عين ما فيه من الكيف والمقدار والشكل، فقد حكموا بأنه قد صدر عن الصورة الواحدة أكثر من أثر واحد وهو عندهم منكر. أو يثبتوا [؟] الجسم حسب ما كان عرض مخصوص صورة مخصوصة، فقد حكموا بإثبات الصور الكثيرة للمادة الواحدة وذلك عندهم ممتنع. لا يقال: هذه الصور مرتبة. لأنا نقول: هذه الأعراض مترتبة فانه ليس بأن [؟] يقال إنصاف النار باليبوسة أولى من العكس فإنا نجد كل واحد منها منفكا عن الأخرى. وإذا [؟] لم يكن بين هذه الآثار ترتب وجب أن لا يكون بين عليها ترتب أيضا فلا تكون تلك الصور مرتبة. ويعود الإشكال. الثانية: الأحكام العامة [؟] وهي أنها [؟] لكل جسم حتى [؟] طبعا. هذا هو المشفر عند المتأخرين. أما أولا فلو قدرناه خاليا عن جميع العوارض المفارقة فإما أن يحصل كل الأمكنة أو لا، وكلاهما محالات. وأما كان معينا فيكون ذلك المكان هو المكان الطبيعي. وقانون [؟] العاشر في الجسم كأنه معينا لكان استدعاؤه له إما أن يكون لجملته أو لوازمه فيكون كل جسم فيه. وإن كان لأمر غير لازم في الجسم فانصاف ذلك الجسم بذلك الأمر إن كان الأمر محالا وبتقدير جوازه فلنجوز أيضا أن يكون الكون له من الأمانة مستحقا له. ثم إنه يحصل على سبيل الاتفاق واحد عنها أو لا. فإن كان مقارنا كان الكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول. أو سابقا عليه معنى أن السابق [؟] اللاحق للاتصاف بالوصف الحاصل. وإذا جاز ذلك فلخزان يقال أيضا إن حصول الجسم في المكان المعين إنما كان لأنه قبل حصول ذلك المكان كان حاصلا في مكان آخر أو موصوفا بصفة أخرى لأجلها يحصل في هذا المكان. وإن لم يكن الاستحقاق الطبيعي حاصلا. ألا ترى أنا إذا دفعنا الحجر إلى فوق عاد؟ ولولا أنه بطبعه طالب المركز وإلا لما كان كذلك. والقائل يقول: لم لا يكون أن يقال إنما عاد لكونه طالبا
The vegetative soul and not any of its concomitants, nor that which is not a state in it nor a locus for it, because all that is false. Even if it were granted, the purpose is achieved, because if the concomitance of those forms were due to these divisions, they would be like the concomitance of shape and quantity. So it is determined that this concomitance is from matter. And if matter is sufficient for the concomitance of the form, why is it not sufficient for the concomitance of these accidents without need to prove these forms? As for the elements, some of them accept shapes easily—that is moist—and some accept with difficulty—that is dry. Since this is the case, why is it not said that easy acceptance of shapes is caused by an existent cause, and difficult acceptance by a non-existent cause, which is the absence of that cause, or conversely? On this estimation, the impossibility of creating bodies is not apparent from the substantial form. Moreover, it is necessary [??] to attribute them to an existent knowledge. So why is it said that that [??] form is an expression of the constitutive state of the subject up to the state that is a cause for the existence of its subject? And if you understand this, then the mentioned proof indicates the causation of these accidents. And there are existent things in the bodies, but there must be an indication that those things are causes for the existence of those bodies, so that those things are considered forms. Otherwise, they are never forms. And I have not yet seen any of them attempt to prove this claim. But granted that the mentioned proof yields the proof of substantial forms, yet here there is something that indicates their negation from two aspects: these forms are either inherent in the body or in the hyle but on condition that the body is inherent in them; on both estimations, their existence is in need of the body. So if the body were caused by them, there would be circularity. Then either they say in the body there is one form that is identical to what is in it of quality, quantity, and shape—they have ruled that more than one effect originates from one form, which is for them denied. Or they establish the body according to a specific accident and a specific form—they have ruled to prove many forms for one matter, and that is for them impossible. It cannot be said: these forms are ordered. For we say: these accidents are ordered, for it is not that [??] the attribution of fire to dryness is more appropriate than the opposite, for we find each of them separate from the other. And if there is no order among these effects, it is necessary that there be no order among them as well, so those forms are not ordered. And the problem returns. Second: the universal judgments [??] that they are for every body until [??] naturally. This is the famous [view] among the later [scholars]. As for first, if we suppose it devoid of all separable accidents, then either it obtains every place or not, both are impossible. And if it is determinate, then that place is the natural place. And the law of the tenth [??] in the body, as if it were determinate, its demand for it would be either due to its totality or its concomitants, so every body would be in it. And if it is due to something non-necessary in the body, then the attribution of that body to that thing, if that thing is impossible, and if its permissibility is granted, let it also be permissible that the existence of it from the trust is worthy of it. Then it occurs by way of coincidence, either one of them or not. If it is concomitant, then the discussion about it is like the first. Or it is prior to it in the sense that the prior [??] the subsequent for being described with the obtained attribute. And if that is permissible, let it be said also that the body's existence in a determinate place was only because, before obtaining that place, it was in another place or described with another attribute for which it obtains this place. Even if natural entitlement is not present. Do you not see that if we push a stone upward, it returns? And were it not that it is by nature a seeker of the center, otherwise it would not be so. And the objector says: why can it not be said that it returns because it is a seeker?
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the doctrine of substantial forms (al-ṣuwar al-nawʿiyya), a key concept in Aristotelian natural philosophy. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) attacks the logicians' proof for the necessity of these forms. He begins by dismissing the idea that forms are needed to explain the concomitance of accidents. He argues that matter itself can account for the connection between shape and magnitude, so why posit additional forms? He then uses the example of elements: moist things accept shapes easily, dry things with difficulty. This difference can be explained by a cause (either existent or absent) without invoking a form. He challenges the notion that the proof for forms actually shows they are causes of the body's existence, noting that no one has demonstrated this. Even if the proof were accepted, he offers a dilemma: either the form is in the body or in matter, but since both need the body, circularity results (body would cause form and vice versa). Moreover, if one form produces multiple effects (like quality, quantity, shape), that violates the principle that one cause yields one effect. If multiple forms are posited for one matter, that is also rejected. The author then refutes the possible defense that forms are ordered, by showing the corresponding accidents are not ordered. Finally, he turns to the concept of natural place (al-makān al-ṭabīʿī), critiquing the later philosophers' idea that bodies have a natural tendency. He argues that if a body is stripped of all accidents, its location is either indeterminate or determinate, both problematic. The movement of a stone upward and returning can be explained by its being a seeker of the center, but this is the very point under debate: whether natural place is intrinsic or acquired. The commentary shows the rigorous dialectical method: the author anticipates objections and systematically undermines the foundational assumptions of the opposing view.

Key Terms
المكان الطبيعي
العوارض المفارقة
الطلب
المركز

Checkpoint

C) Because accidents define location

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the concept of natural place is not logically necessary and can be replaced by explanations relying on accidental attributes.

Analyzing the Celestial Simplicity Argument and Its Refutation
Author refutes the claim that celestial spheres are simple and cannot produce multiple motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises used to argue that celestial motions are uniform and evaluate the logical basis of the refutation.

Core Idea
Simplicity does not entail uniformity when voluntary motion and external relations are considered.

In this passage, the author critiques a commonly held view in astronomy and metaphysics: that celestial spheres, being simple, can only produce one effect. Many scholars considered this a demonstrative premise. However, the author argues that empirical observation of planetary motions shows variation, and that the logical link between simplicity and uniformity is flawed. This lesson will guide you through the argument chain and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
State the pro-simplicity claim
The first argument: the heavens are simple (basit), so they have only one power, producing only one effect. Therefore celestial motions are uniform.
2
State the second argument
The second argument: all motions depend on the Necessary Existence (Wajib al-Wujud), who is unchanging; therefore celestial motions must be unchanging.
3
Identify the author's counter-observations
He notes that voluntary motion (haraka ikhtiyariyya) can admit variation, and we observe differences in speed and distance of planets.
4
Trace the refutation
The author argues that change in celestial motions is not prevented by simplicity because voluntary motion allows variance, and also that terrestrial changes are caused by celestial motions, so change is possible in the celestial realm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يقال في إبطاله من أن أقرب قرب كوكب يساوي أبعد بعد كل الكواكب التي فرضت تحته فقد عرفت ضعف هذا الباب بل يجب أن تكون فوق الفلك التاسع المتحرك بالحركة اليومية من الأفلاك ما لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى. ثم إن الحركات السماوية وجد والاختلافات العارضة لها عادلة وظاهر واحد قوي في ظنونهم أنها في أنفسها غير مختلفة أصلا فلو قيل عليه هذا الاختلاف لوجدوا ولما اختص نظامها بوجه واحد ولما استمرت مشاهدتهم لذلك النظام حكموا بأنها لا تختلف أصلا ما دامت باقية فبنوا على هذا الأصل مباحثهم النجومية. والمتأخرون ظنوا أن هذه المقدمة برهانية والذي يمكن نصرفه به من أصولهم وجهان: الأول: أن السماوات بسيطة فلا تكون لها قوة مختلفة فلا يصدر عنها إلا فعل واحد فالآثار الصادرة عنها لا تختلف. الثاني: أن الحركات الكثيرة تستند إلى واجب الوجود إما بواسطة أو بغير واسطة وعلى التقديرين يلزم من امتناع عروض التغير له امتناع تغيرها. ولا يعارض ذلك بالحوادث الأرضية لأن تغيرها بواسطة الحركات السماوية فلا خرم من تطرق التغير إليها. ثم إن حركات الأفلاك اختيارية والحركة الاختيارية يمنع عروض الاختلاف لها. وقد رأينا في حركات الكواكب اختلافات كثيرة في السرعة والبطء وفي المقادير في الصغر والكبر فعلمنا أن ذلك بسبب اختلاف قربها وبعدها عن الأرض وذلك إنما يكون بسبب الأفلاك لا محالة.
And what is said in its refutation: that the nearest approach of a planet equals the farthest distance of all planets assumed below it—you have recognized the weakness of this approach. Rather, there must be above the ninth sphere moving with the daily motion of the spheres that which only Allah knows. Then the celestial motions are found and the accidental differences are balanced, and a strong apparent unity in their opinions [exists] that they are not different in themselves at all. So if this difference were alleged against them, they would have found [it], and since their system is restricted to one aspect and their observation of that system continued, they ruled that they do not differ at all as long as they remain, and they built their astronomical investigations upon this principle. The later scholars thought this premise is demonstrative, and what can be derived from their principles is two aspects: First, that the heavens are simple, so they do not have different powers, and so only one act proceeds from them, and the effects proceeding from them do not differ. Second, that the many motions depend on the Necessary Existence either through an intermediary or without intermediary, and in both cases the impossibility of change befalling Him necessitates the impossibility of their change. And this is not contradicted by terrestrial events, because their change is through celestial motions, so there is no harm in change reaching them. Then the motions of the spheres are voluntary, and voluntary motion prevents the occurrence of difference in it. And we have seen in the motions of the planets many differences in speed and slowness and in magnitudes large and small; so we know that this is due to the difference of their proximity and distance from the earth, and that is necessarily due to the spheres.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the argument that celestial spheres are simple and therefore produce only uniform motions. The author first points out the weakness of an earlier claim about planetary distances. He then describes how earlier astronomers, observing regularity, assumed celestial motions are unchanging. Later scholars considered this premise demonstrative and gave two reasons: (1) The heavens are simple, so they have only one power producing one effect. (2) All motions depend on the Necessary Being, who is unchanging, so they must be unchanging. The author then refutes these: terrestrial changes are caused by celestial motions, so change is possible. He also notes that voluntary motion can allow variation, and empirical observation shows differences in speed and distance, which must be due to the spheres.

Proof by Division and Elimination
Proof by disjunction and elimination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a demonstrative proof by division and elimination (taqsīm wa tardīd) and identify its disjunctive and categorical syllogistic structure.

Core Idea
Division and elimination yield certainty: what cannot be internal must be external.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses a rigorous logical method to prove a physical fact: the moon's light is borrowed from the sun. He sets up an exhaustive disjunction (cause either external or internal), then eliminates the internal possibility through a categorical syllogism that contradicts observation. This is a classic burhān built on a disjunctive syllogism with categorical sub-arguments. By studying it, you will learn how Islamic logicians applied the syllogism to natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction (taqsīm)
The author divides the possible cause into two: something external to the moon's body or something belonging to it. Write down the two alternatives exactly as stated.
This is an exhaustive and exclusive disjunction (إما...أو).
2
Examine the first alternative (external cause)
The author argues that if the cause were external, it would have to be shadows falling on the moon, but these shadows vary with observer position, which is only explained by sunlight reflecting off the moon. This is presented as a direct observation, not a syllogism, but it establishes that the external cause is not a physical shadow but reflection.
The external cause is actually the sun's light, but the proof refines it.
3
Examine the second alternative (internal cause)
The author eliminates the internal cause with a categorical syllogism: If the moon were self-luminous, it would always appear in one state. But it appears varying (phases). Therefore it is not self-luminous. This is a valid second-figure syllogism: No self-luminous thing varies; the moon varies; therefore the moon is not self-luminous.
This is a categorical syllogism in the second figure (Camestres).
4
Reconstruct the overall argument
Put it together: The cause is either external or internal. The internal is impossible (by the categorical syllogism). Therefore the cause is external, which is reflected sunlight. This is a valid disjunctive syllogism (modus tollendo ponens).
Make sure the disjunction is exhaustive; if there were a third alternative, the argument would be incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إما أن يكون سبب اختلاف أضواء القمر أمرًا خارجًا عن جرمه أو أمرًا عائدًا إليه. والأول باطل، لأن ما يعرض للقمر من وقوع الأشباح فيه مختلف باختلاف مقامات الناظرين، وليس ذلك إلا لانعكاس نور الشمس عنه. والثاني يقتضي أن يكون جرم القمر مضيئًا بذاته، ولو كان كذلك لظهر دائمًا على حالة واحدة، لكنه يظهر مختلفًا، فبطل. فثبت أن نور القمر مستفاد من الشمس.
Either the cause of the moon's varying lights is something external to its body or something belonging to it. The first is false, because what occurs to the moon of the falling of shadows in it differs according to the positions of the observers, and that is only due to the reflection of the sun's light from it. The second requires that the moon's body be luminous by itself, and if it were so it would always appear in one state, but it appears varying, so it is false. Thus it is established that the moon's light is borrowed from the sun.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof by division (taqsīm) and elimination (tardīd) to establish that the moon's light is reflected from the sun, not intrinsic. The author first states the disjunction: the cause of the lunar phases is either external or internal. The external cause is then examined: if external, it would have to be something like shadows falling on the moon, but those shadows vary with the observer's position, which is only explained by sunlight reflecting off the moon. The internal cause (the moon being self-luminous) is refuted by the observed variation: if the moon were intrinsically bright, it would always appear the same, but it does not. Therefore, by elimination, the light is borrowed. This is a standard burhān (demonstration) using categorical reasoning. In Islamic science, this argument was used by astronomers like al-Battānī and Ibn al-Haytham to correct earlier views.

Key Terms
اختلاف أضواء القمر
مستفاد
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Analogical reasoning (tamthīl)
D) Immediate inference ('aks)

Key Takeaway
Demonstrative proof can use disjunction and elimination to reach certainty, provided the partition is exhaustive and the eliminated alternative is impossible.

The Syllogism for Celestial Circular Motion
Natural philosophers’ syllogism for circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism used by natural philosophers to prove that the celestial sphere moves circularly.

Core Idea
A simple body's motion must be circular because it has no inherent direction.

The natural philosophers attempted to prove that the celestial sphere moves by circular motion based on its simplicity. In this lesson, we will reconstruct their reasoning as a formal syllogism and examine its premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the conclusion the natural philosophers aim to prove: 'The celestial sphere moves circularly.'
The conclusion is stated explicitly at the end of their argument.
2
Extract the premises
The argument uses two key premises: (1) The sphere is a simple body (basit). (2) For a simple body, any change of position must be circular because it has no preference for a particular direction.
These premises are linked by conditional reasoning about necessity and possibility.
3
Formulate the syllogism
Combine the premises into a categorical syllogism: Major: All simple bodies capable of change move circularly. Minor: The celestial sphere is a simple body capable of change. Conclusion: Therefore, the celestial sphere moves circularly.
This is a first-figure, Barbara-like syllogism if the terms are properly aligned.
4
Check the validity
If the premises are true, the conclusion follows necessarily. However, the author later challenges the premises, especially the idea that possibility alone implies actual motion.
Validity concerns form, not truth of premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصله أن الأفلاك متحركة، والرياضيون والطبيعيون اختلفوا في سبب حركتها. أما الطبيعيون فذهبوا إلى أن الفلك بسيط، فاختصاصه بوضع معين إما واجب أو جائز. والواجب يستلزم أن يكون لجميع أجزائه وضع واحد، والجائز يقتضي صحة تحرك كل جزء إلى الآخر، وذلك بالحركة المستديرة. وكلما صحت الحركة المستديرة على الفلك، ففيه ميل إلى الاستدارة، وكل ما فيه ميل مستدير فهو متحرك بالاستدارة. فالفلك متحرك بالاستدارة. ثم إن الصحة الحاصلة للقابل لا تعتبر في تحققها المؤثر، بل الصحة المطلقة إنما تثبت بعد حصول القابل والفاعل والترابط وارتفاع الموانع. وإن سلمتم صحة الحركة المستديرة على الفلك بالوجه الأول، فلا يلزم تحقق العلة الفاعلة للحركة. وإن عنيتم الثاني، لم يمكنكم إثبات تلك الصحة إلا بعد شروط منها العلة الفاعلة. وأنتم إنما أثبتم العلة الفاعلة وهي الميل المستدير بواسطة هذه الصحة، فيلزم الدور. ثم قالوا: لو كان الفلك بسيطا لم يكن بأن يحصل بعض أجزائه مع بعض أولى من حصوله مع آخر، فإن ساوى ذلك كان ترجحا بلا مرجح، وإن لم يساو كان ترجحا للممكن على المساوي، وهو حال. وكل متحرك فلابد أن يعرض له تغير بالنسبة إلى جسم آخر، فالفلك كذلك. وذلك الجسم إما خارج عنه أو داخل فيه. وليس خارج الفلك الأقصى جسم آخر، فلا بد أن يكون داخلا فيه، ولا يكون متحركا. فتكون النسبة بين المتحرك والساكن، وهو الأرض في مركزها. ثم زعموا أن الأفلاك كلها إنما تحرك حركاتها البطيئة بحركة فلك الثوابت. والصحيح أن المحرك الآن هو الله تعالى باختياره.
The gist is that the celestial spheres are moving, and the astronomers and natural philosophers disagreed about the cause of their motion. As for the natural philosophers, they held that the sphere is simple, so its specification to a particular position is either necessary or possible. The necessary entails that all its parts have one position, and the possible implies the validity of each part moving to the other, and that by circular motion. And whenever circular motion is valid for the sphere, it has an inclination to circularity, and everything that has a circular inclination moves circularly. So the sphere moves circularly. Then, the possibility residing in the recipient does not require the mover for its existence; rather, absolute possibility is only established after the recipient and the agent come together, along with connection and absence of obstacles. If you admit the validity of circular motion for the sphere in the first sense, then the existence of the efficient cause of motion does not follow. If you mean the second sense, you cannot prove that validity except after conditions including the efficient cause. And you only proved the efficient cause, which is the circular inclination, by means of this validity, resulting in circularity. Then they said: if the sphere were simple, it would not be that some of its parts coming together with some is more fitting than with another. If it is equal, that would be a preponderance without a preponderator; if not equal, it would be a preponderance of the possible over its equal, which is absurd. And every moving thing must undergo change relative to another body; so too the sphere. That body is either outside it or inside it. There is no body outside the outermost sphere, so it must be inside it, and it cannot be moving. So there is a relation between the moving and the stationary, which is the earth at its center. Then they claimed that all the spheres only move their slow motions by the motion of the sphere of fixed stars. The correct view is that the mover now is Allah the Exalted by His choice.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents a complex argument about the motion of celestial spheres. It begins by stating the disagreement between astronomers (riyadiyyun) and natural philosophers (tabi'iyyun) regarding the cause of the spheres' motion. The author then reconstructs the natural philosophers' reasoning: if the sphere is a simple body (basit), then its being in a particular position must be either necessary or possible. If necessary, all parts would have the same position, which is not observed. If possible, then it can move from one position to another, and such motion must be circular because a simple body has no inherent direction. Hence, the sphere has an inclination (mayl) to circular motion, and thus moves circularly. The author then addresses a subtle point: the possibility (sihha) of motion in the sphere (as recipient) does not itself guarantee the existence of an efficient cause (al-'illa al-fa'ila). The possibility is a condition, not a cause. The natural philosophers may have committed circular reasoning by using the possibility to prove the efficient cause (the circular inclination) and then using that cause to explain the possibility. Next, the author presents another argument: if the sphere is simple, it cannot have a preference for one spatial configuration over another without a preponderator (murajjih), otherwise it would be arbitrary. This leads to the conclusion that the sphere must be in motion relative to something else. That something else must be inside the sphere (since nothing outside the outermost sphere). It must be stationary, like the earth. The astronomers claim that all spheres derive their slow motions from the sphere of fixed stars. But the author rejects this, asserting that the true mover is Allah by His free choice (bi-ikhtiyarihi). This aligns with the Ash'ari theological position that all contingent movements are caused by divine will, not by inherent natures. The argument combines physical reasoning with theological commitment, typical of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's approach in the Mulakhkhas.

Key Terms
الفلك
البسيط
الحركة المستديرة
ميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the natural philosophers' argument in this passage, why must the celestial sphere's motion be circular?
A) Because it is made of fire, which rises in a circle.
B) Because it is a simple body with no inherent direction, so any change must be circular.
C) Because the astronomers observed it moving in a circle.
D) Because Allah commanded it to move circularly.

Key Takeaway
The natural philosophers' argument for circular motion relies on the premise that a simple body lacks directional preference, making circular motion the only possible change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the following argument as a categorical syllogism: 'Everything that is temporally originated has a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, the world has a cause.' Identify the major, minor, and middle terms.
?
Find an example from kalam where a conclusion is drawn from the nature of a simple body, and test whether the syllogism is valid.

The Circular Reasoning Fallacy in the Natural Philosophers' Proof
Author’s critique of circular reasoning in the natural philosophers' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the circular reasoning (dawr) that Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uncovers in the natural philosophers' proof for the efficient cause of celestial motion.

Core Idea
Using the possibility of motion to prove its cause, then using that cause to explain the possibility, is circular.

After presenting the natural philosophers' syllogism, the author points out a logical flaw: they use the possibility of circular motion to infer the existence of a circular inclination (the efficient cause), but then they need that cause to establish the possibility. This is a classic case of circular reasoning (dawr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two steps in the natural philosophers' reasoning
First, they claim that because circular motion is valid for the sphere, it has an inclination (mayl) to circular motion. Second, they use this inclination to explain why the sphere actually moves circularly.
The possibility (sihha) of motion is presented as evidence for the cause (the inclination).
2
Locate the author's objection
The author states: 'The possibility residing in the recipient does not require the mover for its existence; rather, absolute possibility is only established after the recipient and the agent come together.' This means the possibility itself depends on the existence of the efficient cause.
The author distinguishes between conditional possibility (which does not need a cause) and absolute possibility (which does).
3
Reconstruct the circularity
If the possibility is taken as absolute, then proving it requires already having the efficient cause. But they use the possibility to prove the efficient cause. Thus, the proof assumes what it tries to prove.
The circularity is between the possibility (sihha) and the efficient cause (the circular inclination).
4
Assess the fallacy
This is a form of begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub). The natural philosophers cannot simultaneously use possibility to prove the cause and the cause to explain possibility without circularity.
The author's critique effectively undermines the natural philosophers' argument for the cause of celestial motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصله أن الأفلاك متحركة، والرياضيون والطبيعيون اختلفوا في سبب حركتها. أما الطبيعيون فذهبوا إلى أن الفلك بسيط، فاختصاصه بوضع معين إما واجب أو جائز. والواجب يستلزم أن يكون لجميع أجزائه وضع واحد، والجائز يقتضي صحة تحرك كل جزء إلى الآخر، وذلك بالحركة المستديرة. وكلما صحت الحركة المستديرة على الفلك، ففيه ميل إلى الاستدارة، وكل ما فيه ميل مستدير فهو متحرك بالاستدارة. فالفلك متحرك بالاستدارة. ثم إن الصحة الحاصلة للقابل لا تعتبر في تحققها المؤثر، بل الصحة المطلقة إنما تثبت بعد حصول القابل والفاعل والترابط وارتفاع الموانع. وإن سلمتم صحة الحركة المستديرة على الفلك بالوجه الأول، فلا يلزم تحقق العلة الفاعلة للحركة. وإن عنيتم الثاني، لم يمكنكم إثبات تلك الصحة إلا بعد شروط منها العلة الفاعلة. وأنتم إنما أثبتم العلة الفاعلة وهي الميل المستدير بواسطة هذه الصحة، فيلزم الدور. ثم قالوا: لو كان الفلك بسيطا لم يكن بأن يحصل بعض أجزائه مع بعض أولى من حصوله مع آخر، فإن ساوى ذلك كان ترجحا بلا مرجح، وإن لم يساو كان ترجحا للممكن على المساوي، وهو حال. وكل متحرك فلابد أن يعرض له تغير بالنسبة إلى جسم آخر، فالفلك كذلك. وذلك الجسم إما خارج عنه أو داخل فيه. وليس خارج الفلك الأقصى جسم آخر، فلا بد أن يكون داخلا فيه، ولا يكون متحركا. فتكون النسبة بين المتحرك والساكن، وهو الأرض في مركزها. ثم زعموا أن الأفلاك كلها إنما تحرك حركاتها البطيئة بحركة فلك الثوابت. والصحيح أن المحرك الآن هو الله تعالى باختياره.
The gist is that the celestial spheres are moving, and the astronomers and natural philosophers disagreed about the cause of their motion. As for the natural philosophers, they held that the sphere is simple, so its specification to a particular position is either necessary or possible. The necessary entails that all its parts have one position, and the possible implies the validity of each part moving to the other, and that by circular motion. And whenever circular motion is valid for the sphere, it has an inclination to circularity, and everything that has a circular inclination moves circularly. So the sphere moves circularly. Then, the possibility residing in the recipient does not require the mover for its existence; rather, absolute possibility is only established after the recipient and the agent come together, along with connection and absence of obstacles. If you admit the validity of circular motion for the sphere in the first sense, then the existence of the efficient cause of motion does not follow. If you mean the second sense, you cannot prove that validity except after conditions including the efficient cause. And you only proved the efficient cause, which is the circular inclination, by means of this validity, resulting in circularity. Then they said: if the sphere were simple, it would not be that some of its parts coming together with some is more fitting than with another. If it is equal, that would be a preponderance without a preponderator; if not equal, it would be a preponderance of the possible over its equal, which is absurd. And every moving thing must undergo change relative to another body; so too the sphere. That body is either outside it or inside it. There is no body outside the outermost sphere, so it must be inside it, and it cannot be moving. So there is a relation between the moving and the stationary, which is the earth at its center. Then they claimed that all the spheres only move their slow motions by the motion of the sphere of fixed stars. The correct view is that the mover now is Allah the Exalted by His choice.

The Argument from Simplicity: Why a Preponderator is Needed
Author’s argument that simplicity implies need for a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the argument that the simplicity of the celestial sphere necessitates a preponderator (murajjih), and understand its implications for the existence of a mover.

Core Idea
If a simple body has no preference, its actual state requires a preponderator to avoid absurdity.

The natural philosophers claimed that the celestial sphere's simplicity explains its circular motion. But the author turns this simplicity into an argument against their naturalism: if the sphere is truly simple and homogeneous, then its being in one configuration rather than another would be arbitrary without a preponderator. This leads to the conclusion that the sphere's motion requires an external agent of choice.

Guided Path
1
State the premise about simplicity
The sphere is a simple body (basit), meaning all its parts are identical in nature. Therefore, it has no inherent preference for one spatial arrangement over another.
This is the same premise used by the natural philosophers, but now used against them.
2
Analyze the disjunction
If the sphere is in a particular configuration, either it is equal for all parts to be in that configuration or not. If equal, then there is no reason for that configuration, so a preponderator is needed. If not equal, then there is a preference, which contradicts simplicity.
Thus, either way, a preponderator (murajjih) is required to explain why the sphere is as it is.
3
Connect to motion
Since the sphere is moving, its positions change. Each change requires a preponderator to pick one position over another. This preponderator cannot be part of the sphere itself (since it is simple), so it must be external.
The argument leads to the need for an external mover that acts by choice, not by nature.
4
Evaluate the argument
The argument is a classic instance of the kalam principle of preponderance (tarjih). It assumes that without a preponderator, any state of affairs is arbitrary and impossible. This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari proofs for God's existence.
The argument does not yet identify the preponderator as Allah, but it sets the stage for that conclusion.

Source Text

The True Mover: Allah by Choice
Author’s conclusion that Allah is the mover by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the final conclusion of the passage—that the mover of the celestial spheres is Allah by choice—and reconstruct the arguments that lead to this conclusion.

Core Idea
The celestial spheres' motion ultimately proves that Allah is the mover, acting by free will, not by natural necessity.

After critiquing the natural philosophers' explanations, the author presents the correct view: the celestial spheres are moved by Allah through His free choice (ikhtiyar). This conclusion is reached by eliminating natural causes and showing that the spheres' motion requires a voluntary agent.

Guided Path
1
Recall the problems with natural explanations
The natural philosophers' arguments for circular motion and its cause were shown to be fallacious (circular reasoning) and insufficient (need for preponderator).
This clears the ground for the theological explanation.
2
Identify the reference point argument
The author argues that every moving thing must change position relative to something else. For the outermost sphere, that something must be inside it (since nothing outside). That inside body must be stationary, like the earth.
This establishes a relative reference, but does not yet identify the mover.
3
Refute the astronomers' claim
The astronomers claimed that all spheres derive their motion from the sphere of fixed stars. The author rejects this, implying that it merely postpones the problem.
The chain of motion cannot regress infinitely; it must end in a first mover.
4
State the final conclusion
The correct view is that the mover now is Allah the Exalted by His choice. This means the motion is not due to inherent nature (tab') but due to divine will.
This is the Ash'ari position: all contingent motions are directly caused by Allah.

Source Text
